Received 
      10th October 2022
    First published on 5th July 2023AbstractDiversification of polymer waste recycling is one of the solutions to improve the current environmental scenario. Upcycling is a promising strategy for converting polymer waste into molecular intermediates and high-value products. Although the catalytic transformations into small molecules have been actively discussed, the methods and characteristics of upcycling into new materials have not yet been addressed. Recently, the functionalisation of polymer wastes (polyethylene terephthalate bottles, polypropylene surgical masks, rubber tires, etc.) and their conversion into new materials with enhanced functionality have been proposed as an appealing alternative for dealing with polymer waste recycling/treatment. In this review, the term 'functional upcycling' is introduced to designate any method of post-polymerisation modification or surface functionalisation without considerable polymer chain destruction to produce a new upcycled material with added value. This review explores the functional upcycling strategy with detailed consideration of the most common polymers, i.e., polystyrene, poly(methyl methacrylate), polyethylene, polypropylene, polyurethane, polyethylene terephthalate, polyvinyl chloride, polycarbonate, and rubber. We discuss the composition of plastic waste, reactivity, available physical/chemical agents for modification, and the interconnection between their properties and application. To date, upcycled materials have been successfully applied as adsorbents (including CO2), catalysts, electrode materials for energy storage and sensing, demonstrating a high added value. Importantly, the reviewed reports indicated that the specific performance of upcycled materials is generally comparable or higher than that of similar materials prepared from virgin polymer feedstock. All these advantages promote functional upcycling as a promising diversification approach against the common postprocessing methods employed for polymer waste. Finally, to identify the limitations and suggest future scope of research for each polymer, we comparatively analysed the aspects of functional upcycling with those of chemical and mechanical recycling, considering the energy and resource costs, toxicity of the used chemicals, environmental footprint, and the value added to the product.
Olga Guselnikova
Dr Olga Guselnikova received her PhD degree in chemistry from the University of Chemistry and Technology Prague (Czech Republic) and Tomsk Polytechnic University (Russia) in 2019. She is a fellow of Japan Society for the Promotion of Science at the National Institute for Materials Science, Japan, and a sub-group leader of “functional materials” at Tomsk Polytechnic University. Her research interests are related to surface chemistry for functional materials. This means that she is applying her background in organic chemistry to materials science: plasmonic and polymer surfaces are hybridized with organic molecules to create high-performance elements and devices.
Yusuke Yamauchi
Yusuke Yamauchi received his PhD degree from Waseda University in 2007 and subsequently established his research group at the National Institute of Materials Science (NIMS). In 2017, he moved to the University of Queensland where he currently serves as a professor and senior group leader at the School of Chemical Engineering and the Australian Institute for Bioengineering and Nanotechnology (AIBN). He is also a distinguished professor at Nagoya University and an ERATO Research Director at the JST-ERATO Yamauchi Materials Space-Tectonics. His area of expertise is inorganic material nanoarchitectonics.
Rabah Boukherroub
Dr Rabah Boukherroub received his PhD degree in chemistry from the University Paul Sabatier in Toulouse, France. He is currently a research director at the French National Center for Scientific Research (CNRS) and a group leader at the Institute of Electronics, Microelectronics and Nanotechnology (IEMN), Lille, France. He is an Editor for ACS Applied Materials & Interfaces. His research interests are in the area of functional materials, surface chemistry, and photophysics of semiconductor/metal nanostructures with emphasis on biosensors, nanomedicine, photocatalysis and electrocatalysis. He is also a co-founder of BioNaMeris, a biotech firm specialized in sustainable aquaculture.
Pavel Postnikov
Prof. Pavel S. Postnikov completed his doctorate in organic chemistry with theoretical and practical insights in diazonium chemistry at the Tomsk Polytechnic University, Russia, in 2011. In 2021, he successfully passed the habilitation procedure at the Novosibirsk Institute of Organic Chemistry, RAS, Russia (organic and physical chemistry). He serves as an associate editor of Chemistry Africa journal. His research interests are plastic recycling, composite materials, surface chemistry, plasmon catalysis, hypervalent iodine, and diazonium salts.
1. Introduction
Plastic-based products have become deeply ingrained in daily life activities owing to their intensive usage in almost every aspect of human life (packaging, vehicles, medicine, etc.). However, the widespread mismanagement of plastic products has resulted in environmental pollution because their usage lifespan is several times shorter than their decomposition period or end-of-life cycle. In 2019, the total amount of polymer waste reached 370 million tons (Mt).1,2 Moreover, plastic pollution has become unavoidable because of the lifestyle transformations induced by the COVID-19 pandemic. During this challenging period, the extensive usage of personal protective equipment (e.g., masks, gloves, and suits) and the packaging materials in food and goods delivery resulted in extremely high growth of plastic waste. Consequently, in 2021, 11 Mt of plastic waste was solely attributed to the pandemic.3 Therefore, the forecasted amount of polymer waste by 2050 was not on track, and the amount of plastic waste can increase by up to 1100 Mt annually.4,5 According to,2 less than 10% of plastic waste is recycled, making the related issues especially remarkable. To this end, the scientific progress in the field of plastic recycling has achieved promising results. Thus, several review articles and books have been published to provide a general overview of the basic issues of plastic recycling: identification, sorting, separation, and environmental and economic aspects.6–11 Despite the immense significance of these issues, new experimental strategies should be discovered to reverse the momentum and render polymer recycling advantageous.
In this regard, a careful analysis of the existing research literature enables the classification of methods employed in polymer management into several general strategies (Fig. 1). The most essential parameters for selecting an appropriate polymer recycling/treatment strategy include energy and resource costs, environmental impact (toxicity of used chemicals, CO2 release, and environmental poisoning by microplastics (MP)), and the added value of the product.
 Fig. 1  Classification of strategies for polymer waste treatment based on C–X/C–C bond cleavage and analysis of each strategy: (a) landfilling; (b) mechanical recycling; (c) functional upcycling; (d) chemical re-/upcycling; and (e) incineration. 
The negative impact of waste on the environment should be minimised at the processing stage and the amount of energy and chemicals should be reduced, especially considering that the production of virgin polymers consumes energy and generates a carbon footprint. However, the most common strategies of polymer waste management – incineration and landfilling – do not completely follow this concept. Although incineration can partially recover energy in a short time, such processes induce significant negative environmental effects.12,13 Additionally, these processes release solid micro/nanoparticles containing toxic elements that have been recognised as hazardous to the environment.14 Nevertheless, this strategy is beneficial in comparison to landfilling because it provides opportunity for particular energy recovery from polymer waste incineration. Although landfills do not emit greenhouse gases, the plastic waste persists on the earth in an unrecycled form,15 thereby creating health and environmental issues. Thus, both these primitive strategies – incineration and landfilling – do not generate high-value products from polymer waste and have a significant negative effect on the surrounding environment. Therefore, a balance should be maintained between energy and chemical consumption, CO2 emissions, and increased added value of products. The latest reviews have underlined the significance of high-value recycled materials within the circular economy framework.14,16,17
According to extensive research and published articles on polymer waste management, the strategies can be classified into four categories: (i) primary (landfilling); (ii) secondary (mechanical); (iii) tertiary (chemical), and (iv) quaternary (incineration for energy recovery) (Fig. 1).18 Alternatively, suggesting from a chemical perspective, these strategies could be classified based on the bond (C–X/C–C) cleavage degree in the primary polymer chain (Fig. 1), which is directly related to the amount of CO2 released, MP poisoning, and energy and resource costs. According to the proposed classification, the C–X/C–C bond cleavage proceeds extremely slowly during landfilling and the polymer waste remains on the planet in its original form without any utilisation or practical purpose (Fig. 1a).
In contrast to landfilling (Fig. 1e), incineration enables energy restoration; however, the energy requirement should be considered for all processes including the complete degradation of C–X/C–C bonds in polymer chains (for instance, 80–140 kcal mol−1 for C–C/CC bonds).19 Previously, recycling polymer waste conserved 2–3 times more energy than that generated by incinerating plastic waste.11 Moreover, incineration releases greenhouse gases and toxins, making it the least environmentally friendly strategy.
Moving forward to mechanical recycling, this strategy involves polymer waste transformation into lower quality polymer products through mechanical processes such as melting, re-granulating, grinding, and compounding (Fig. 1b).20–22 Alternatively, mechanically processed polymer waste can be combined with other materials such as sand and wood to create new building materials.23–26 However, the raw plastic waste needs to be highly pure and uniform.22 As a result, mechanical recycling does not release CO2 (or releases a negligible amount) because of the extremely low degree of C–X/C–C bond cleavage, which is beneficial for the environment. However, polymer quality deteriorates due to the shortening of the polymer chain via thermal treatment during processing.27–29 Nonetheless, this method does not resolve the issues of recycling polymer mixtures and thermosets. In particular, its major limitations include deteriorated properties of final products, technological complexity of processing, and low added value of building materials.
The opposite approach to mechanical recycling is chemical treatment, which involves the partial destruction of the C–X/C–C bonds, i.e., depolymerisation of polymers to monomers or preparation of new small molecules (Fig. 1d).11,14,30 Although depolymerisation with subsequent repolymerisation yields polymer products with improved quality compared with mechanically recycled ones, the full cleavage of C–X/C–C bonds requires a higher amount of energy, especially for polyolefins, or utilisation of expensive catalysts.14,22,31 Despite the high added value of monomers and new small molecules (chemical additives, waxes, and lubricants),14,32,33 the energy and feedstock cost of chemical recycling raises doubts on the financial efficiency of this model.
1.1. Upcycling concept: catalytic techniques versus material design
In search of a more value-effective approach for solving the polymer waste problem, Gunter Pauli introduced the definition of 'upcycling' in 1999,34 which has recently gained popularity. According to ref. 11 and 32, chemical upcycling is an approach in which new value-added molecules, polymers, or materials are prepared through chemical methods and/or functional (surface) modification of the parent polymer. For instance, thermochemical catalytic methods such as pyrolysis and thermal cracking32,33,35 are utilised to prepare value-added fuels and hydrocarbon feedstock (Fig. 1d and 2). In this case, complete polymer chain destruction occurs, which requires a substantial amount of energy. The fundamental principles and trends of catalysis can be reasonably applied to these transformations.36 In contrast to catalytic techniques leading to depolymerisation, only partial C–X/C–C bond cleavage or post-polymerisation modification is required for the preparation of new materials.
 Fig. 2  Comparison of chemical and functional upcycling and obtained products. 
The design of functional materials to some extent differs from the principles of catalysis that require complete depolymerisation (Fig. 2). In catalysis, the complete C–X/C–C bond cleavage requires homogenous organocatalysts37,38 or complex heterogeneous catalytic systems utilising rare-earth metals (e.g., Pd and Ru) along with expensive ligands39,40 and long reaction period to attain high conversion and selectivity. Despite catalytic fundamentals being important, post-polymerisation modification and surface chemistry principles are dominant for functional upcycling (Fig. 2). If chemical upcycling generates new molecules, for the design of functional materials, the intrinsic properties of the primary polymer chain should be preserved, and its properties are tailored for specific applications. The distinction between chemical and functional upcycling has not been clearly defined previously, as functional upcycling is a relatively recent development and lacks detailed formulation.
For instance, the surface of waste polyethylene terephthalate (PET) bottles,41 polypropylene (PP) surgical masks,42 tyre rubber,43 and polyvinyl chloride (PVC) tubes44 were chemically treated to generate new functional groups with partial cleavage of C–X/C–C bonds. However, the major component of the bulk polymer remained conserved. Furthermore, the treated PET, PP, rubber and PVC were transformed into adsorbents of insecticides and ions.41–43 The practical implementation of this strategy is relatively affordable as it requires low energy resources, does not release CO2, and provides a considerably high added value. It represents a considerable example of a balance between environmental pollution, resources, and added value.
1.2. Introducing functional upcycling
Based on the above discussion, we introduce a new term – 'functional upcycling' – in the framework of this review (Fig. 1c and 2). Functional upcycling of polymers is a new method of post-polymerisation modification or surface functionalisation without sufficient polymer chain (C–X/C–C) cleavage, and the new upcycled material exhibits specific enhanced properties compared with the pristine polymer. Functional upcycling converts polymeric waste by altering the surface properties or transformations of the primary polymer chain to produce new materials for specific applications. The additional functional groups are immobilised onto the polymer surface via covalent or noncovalent bonding (e.g., hydrophobic interactions, hydrogen bonds, and van der Waals forces). The functional upcycling strategy aims to repurpose waste plastics into materials with additional functions and value. In this case, the resulting material such as a sorbent, catalyst, antimicrobial material, or membrane may provide a higher added value than the fuel or the original polymer (Fig. 3).45–48
 Fig. 3  Generalised concept of functional upcycling. 
The following requirements should be fulfilled to realise ‘functional upcycling’:
1. Functional upcycling applies chemical, physical and/or biological methodologies to post-polymerisation modification or surface modification without sufficient decomposition of the main polymer chains in bulk material;
2. During functional upcycling, a major part of the resulting material is a waste polymer with the conservation of primary polymeric chains;
3. The presence of additional functional groups should be confirmed by at least one analytical method;
4. Functional upcycling yields a new material with a specific function, or at least, high potential for applications;
5. The prepared material should exhibit an added value.
Owing to partial C–X/C–C bond cleavage in functional upcycling, the reagents and solvent costs along with energy costs (due to reduced heating and additional pressure) are reduced. Moreover, both CO2 emissions and MP release could be suppressed. Milder functionalisation conditions for polymer waste reduce the cost of the process and increase the added value of the upcycled material (Fig. 1c and 3). Upcycled materials are aimed at solving problems in various life cycles, for instance, sorbents can be used for capturing ecotoxicants,43,49 which contributes positively to the environment. Based on circular economy aspects, environmentally-friendly and cost-effective approaches have emerged as a new trend for solving polymer waste problems.14,17
To realize functional upcycling of plastic waste, each stage of waste disposal needs to be addressed. Thus far, the most commonly reported sources for functional upcycling include postconsumer wastes such as PET bottles,41 PP face masks,42,50,51 polystyrene (PS) foam, and rubber tyres.43 Despite the challenges involved in sorting postconsumer waste for functional upcycling owing to the complex mixture of food and other waste plastics, researchers generally are prone to use such waste after a simple precleaning process owing to its high availability in laboratory experiments. Upon addressing the challenge of scaling up functional upcycling, more convenient and cleaner post-industrial waste plastics can be tested. Regardless, the waste should be sorted, pre-treated, or even preliminarily undergo mechanical treatment. In addition to surface properties, the initial size and shape of plastic waste must be adapted to ensure the applicability of the value-added product. After shaping plastic to the required state, it should undergo functional upcycling. Commonly, the bulk contamination from plastic waste is removed by cleaning with water/ethanol/acetone and drying. Furthermore, depending on the polymer and intended application, the treatment methods can be categorized into surface functionalization, or bulk modification through post-polymerisation processes. In the case of surface treatment, waste can be used with minimal pre-treatment,47,52 manually machined to centimetre-scale pieces,53,54 extruded to produce granulates or powder,43,55 or shaped into fibers by electrospinning.56 Alternatively, plastic waste is dissolved/treated with reagents for functionalisation,57–59 relevant organic solvents, or their mixtures such as dichloromethane,45,59 chloroform, xylene/toluene51,60 with the addition of ethyl acetate and hexane.61
In this review, we provide an overview of the 'functional upcycling' strategy with detailed consideration of the most common polymers (PS, poly(methyl methacrylate) (PMMA), PE, PP, polyurethane (PU), PET, PVC, polycarbonate (PC), and rubber). We reviewed these polymers (PET, PE, PVC, PP, and PS) according to their plastic code numbering, starting with PET involved in a number of reactions owing to the presence of aryl and ester groups. Furthermore, we overviewed PU and PMMA as they bear specific codes. Finally, we end the discussion with less common polymers, e.g., rubber and PC, which are promising for functional upcycling. For each polymer, we briefly introduce its mechanical and chemical re-/upcycling strategies to identify the challenges and comparatively analyse their potential for functional upcycling (discussed in ESI†). Thereafter, we analyse the structure of each polymer and its reactivity, which enabled us to consider the possible surface transformation for both new and waste polymers. We discuss the recent examples of functional upcycling, focusing on the application of upcycled products for each polymer. Moreover, we reviewed the functionalisation strategies of certain commercial polymers that can be successfully utilised for the functional upcycling of wastes. The reported examples can serve as a guide for readers interested in exploring the first attempts of functional upcycling of these polymers. More importantly, we compared the functionality and applicability of the recycled products with those of the recently reported analogues prepared from new materials to conclude the perspectives of functional upcycling strategy. Finally, functional upcycling was evaluated for industrial implementation and compared with mechanical and chemical re-/upcycling.
2. Overview of the functional upcycling strategy for different polymers
2.1. Polyethylene terephthalate (PET)

2.1.1. General remarks. 
PET is the most common thermoplastic polymer resin of the polyester family, and it is widely used in the production of clothing, water-storage tanks, and food-related products.62–64 The widespread applicability of PET is attributed to its mechanical strength65 combined with hydrophobicity,66 transparency,67 thermoplasticity, thermal stability,68 and availability.69
In 2019, 30.5 Mt of PET was produced, and in 2024, this number is expected to increase up to 35 Mt.70 Although PET can be considered an ideal recyclable plastic, only 30% of all PET waste is recycled every year,71,72 and the remaining 70% is disposed in landfills, incinerated, or becomes a contaminant and source of MP.73
The relevant choice of recycling approaches for PET wastes currently encounters challenges related to the properties of virgin PET,62–64,74 which are altered by in-use aging and the presence of additives depending on the previous application.75 As such, the structural characteristics of waste PET plastic may deteriorate over time under exposure to solar radiation, temperature,76 and mechanical stress.77 In particular, the physical properties such as morphology, crystallinity, and surface area are primarily affected by aging.78 The variations in chemical structures during aging result in the formation of hydroxyl groups via hydrolysis79,80 and cyclic oligomers, especially through thermal degradation. To prevent aging, various additives are applied: ultraviolet (UV) stabilisers (benzophenone and benzotriazole),81 antioxidants (boric acid and brominated flame retardants), lead compounds, amides of fatty acids, and pigments.82 However, the composition of the PET plastic waste should be determined to prevent the occurrence of undesired variations in the reactivity of the catalytic systems applied for re-/upcycling and to control the possible leakage during re-/upcycling. Regardless, the normal aging process can lead to the formation of the required functional groups for further modification, thereby rendering the upcycling strategies more desirable in comparison with chemical re-/upcycling.
Based on related reviews, the common mechanical83–85 (Section S1.1 in ESI†) and chemical re-/upcycling86–88 (Section S1.2 in ESI†) techniques are briefly discussed in Section S1 (ESI†), and the functional upcycling method is compared with other methods of PET waste recycling.


2.1.2. Functional upcycling. 
2.1.2.1. Structure of the polymer, active groups, and functionalisation agents. 
PET is a product of condensation between ethylene glycol (EG) and terephthalic acid (TA). The simplest technique of functional upcycling of waste PET is the deposition of an additional layer via noncovalent interactions. For instance, the dip-coating of PET membranes onto polydimethylsiloxane (PDMS) provides superoleophilic and superhydrophobic properties to the material.89 A coating of 1H,1H,2H,2H-perfluorodecyltriethoxysilane was applied for the creation of membranes for distillation.90 Despite the questionable stability of noncovalent coatings, the authors demonstrated excellent recycling ability up to 10 times for oil–water separation. In particular, the membranes for oil–water separation were prepared from waste PET through in situ deposition of ZnO nanoparticles (NPs) and the reduction of zinc nitrate, followed by the modification with hydrophobic groups to increase affinity toward oils.91,92
Although noncovalent methods can be easily implemented, the resulting materials suffer from poor stability of the coating. Therefore, covalent methods are expected to produce more stable materials. In general, most methods that introduce specific properties to the PET surface are associated with the conversion of the polymer backbone to the available reactive groups by treating with nucleophilic species similar to chemical recycling (Section S1.2 in ESI†) (Fig. 4a). Note that physical methods such as plasma treatment have been investigated for the surface activation and release of active –OH/–COOH groups.93–96
 Fig. 4  Functionalisation of PET: (a) surface hydrolysis;93–96 (b) growth of metal–organic framework;41,97,98 (c) surface aminolysis;99–102 (d) free radical polymerisation;53,103–105 (e) atom transfer radical polymerisation;106–108 (f) transesterification46,109 and (g) hydroarylation.110 
Surface hydrolysis and growth of metal–organic frameworks. 
The major product of PET hydrolysis is TA87 which is widely used for preparing an enormous family of metal–organic frameworks (MOFs). The concept of applying PET for preparing MOFs triggered a fascinating novel direction in polymer re-/upcycling by combining the modern trends in chemical science.111 Indeed, in the case of preparation of powdered MOFs, complete hydrolysis of the polymer backbone is required, which relates such approaches to chemical recycling (Section S1.2 in ESI†). Nevertheless, we discuss the preparation of MOFs by chemical recycling from waste PET because of the following reasons. First reason is that MOFs are functional compounds that serve materials in the adsorption/separation and decomposition of toxins.111–113 Secondly, MOFs are of vital importance in science and technology because of their high surface area and controlled porosity.114–116 BASF company successfully markets MIL-53(Al) type terephthalate MOFs, which is a considerable example showing the increasing demand for MOFs.117 However, the large-scale production of TA by common oxidation of petroleum-derived xylenes is currently limited by high expenditure.
The TA derived from PET hydrolysis can be utilised to prepare several types of terephthalate-based MOFs such as MIL (Materials Institute Lavoisier) and UiO (University of Oslo). For instance, an MIL-type was produced by heating an aqueous mixture of waste PET chips to 200 °C in the presence of trivalent metal salts (e.g., AlCl3·6H2O, VCl3, CrCl3·6H2O, Al(NO3)3·9H2O, Ga(NO3)3·9H2O, and CrCl3·6H2O). The metal salts served as sources of metal ions for MOFs and accelerated the depolymerisation of PET. This strategy enabled the successful synthesis of the powders of MIL-type MOFs.118–124 In addition, a range of UiO-66 powders was prepared by in situ hydrolysis and ZrO2 addition,123 and a detailed review of synthesising MOFs from PET has been recently published.111,124
In this context, de Vos et al. applied surface hydrolysis to release carboxylic groups on PET surfaces without complete polymer chain destruction and transformed the final product from powdered MOFs to PET-based materials.97,125 They extended the aforementioned procedure to grow MIL-53(Al) and UiO-66(Zr) as a reactor on the waste PET bottle itself. Consequently, MOFs were directly formed on the surface of hydrolysed PET by binding the released carboxylic groups on the surface (Fig. 4b).97 Subsequently, our research group improved the procedure: acid hydrolysis was used for partial depolymerisation of waste PET chips for further growth of UiO-66 on the surface. We prepared a PET-based hybrid material with covalently bound UiO-66 on the surface for the adsorptive removal,41 catalytic degradation of toxins,98 separation of aromatic chlorinated contaminants125 or subsequent carbonization for improved photothermal properties.126 Recently, Barakzehi et al. used commercial PET fibers for the surface-assisted growth of Al2(BDC)3 for flexible supercapacitor application. PET fabric was pre-treated with NaOH aqueous solution to partially depolymerise the surface of the PET fibers, followed by MOF formation.127 This technology could be easily transferred to waste PET fabric.
Surface aminolysis. 
The surface aminolysis involves the interaction of hydrolysed or non-hydrolysed PET surfaces with aliphatic amines such as 4-amino-1-butanol,128 diethylenetriamine (DTA),101 ethylenediamine (EDA),99,100,102 triethylenetetramine,129,130 under catalytic conditions (sodium/zinc acetate or bases) or simple heating (Fig. 4c and Table 1(4)). Surface aminolysis can be also realized via NH3 plasma treatment101 (Table 1(4)). Amino groups formed during aminolysis can serve as active centres for further organic transformations (Fig. 4c and Table 1(5)),131–133 and enhancing the antibacterial properties and biocompatibility.93,133,134

Surface polymerisation. 
Simple radical polymerisation has been extensively performed on PET fabric, fibers, and films. To generate radicals on the PET surface, it was treated with γ-irradiation,103,104 ozone,53 or peroxides.105 This technology enables the polymerisation of common monomers such as vinyl acetate103 and acrylic acid (AA).104
Nonetheless, the number of monomers and structures of the resulting polymers is substantially limited for radical processes. Therefore, more advanced atom transfer radical polymerisation (ATRP) has been applied for post-polymerisation modification of waste PET. ATRP is characterized by a large selection of monomers, initiators, and control over the polymer structure. In the case of PET, surface ATRP has been carried out by preliminary covalent grafting of 3-aminopropyltriethoxysilane (APTES) to couple with the bromoalkyl initiator, followed by the growth of poly(2-dimethylaminoethyl methacrylate) and poly(2-hydroxyethyl methacrylate) brushes (Fig. 4e).106,107 Alternatively, temperature-responsive poly(N-isopropylacrylamide) (PNIPAM) was polymerised using the same strategy106 to ensure the suitability of PET for filtration materials, tissue scaffolds, and sensors requiring robust microfibers. Recently, more functional statistical copolymers composed of poly(butyl acrylate) and poly(styren-azobenzene) were grafted by ATRP to prepare a photoresponsive material.108
Other chemical transformations. 
As described previously in chemical recycling (Section S1.2 in ESI†), PET can be transformed into appropriate functional derivatives by surface transesterification. For instance, poly(L-lactic acid) moieties were introduced to the waste PET surface in o-nitrophenol as a solvent in the presence of dibutyltin oxide as a catalyst.109 Other common transesterification agents include phenols (phenol, 4-chlorophenol, and hydroxyquinone) at temperatures higher than the phenol melting point (Fig. 4f and Table 1(6)).46
Recently, Azoulay et al. demonstrated a new method for PET post-polymerisation functionalisation: a gold-catalysed intermolecular hydroarylation of PET with alkynes. This catalytic condition enables direct functionalisation of phenylene rings with 1,2-substituted methyl acrylate (Fig. 4g and Table 1(7)).110 Although this process is advantageous in terms of high chemoselectivity and mild conditions, the catalyst preparation is complicated. The functionalised products exhibited significant variations of their important physical properties such as the tangent modulus, melt viscosity, and wettability. The correlations between Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) and nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) data were consistent with the high chemoselectivity of the process.
The most common methods for characterizing the synthesised materials include FTIR, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), and other spectroscopic methods. The chemical functionalisation of polymer backbones has been commonly demonstrated using NMR spectroscopy. Finally, the formation of hybrid materials with MOFs requires additional characterization by X-ray diffraction (XRD) to validate the crystallinity of the MOF phases.
2.1.2.2. Application of functional materials and comparison with previously reported materials. 
The functional upcycling of PET waste produces new high-performance materials for antibacterial applications, sorbent and catalyst technologies, and design of materials with enhanced mechanical properties. In contrast to other polymer waste (e.g., PVC and rubber), functional upcycling of PET into functional materials has garnered considerable research attention.
Antibacterial applications. 
The functional upcycling of waste PET enables the design of materials with antibacterial and sterilization properties via plasma-induced aminolysis,101 surface-assisted polyacrylic acid (PAA) polymerisation,104 or incorporation of zinc oxide NPs.91,92 Despite various action mechanisms, the performance of these materials is not inferior to that of similar materials prepared from virgin PET.135,136 Nevertheless, the application of plastic waste for medical devices in contact with the human body is elusive owing to the absence of regulatory documentation. Thus, functionally upcycled PET has been applied in certain alternative purposes. For instance, upcycled waste PET can be used for food packaging according to recently established regulations,137 and production of textiles138,139 and sportswear.140
Adsorbents. 
One of the main applications of upcycled PET is adsorption and removal of a wide range of metal ions (Cr3+, Ni2+, Cu2+, Pb2+, Zn2+, and Hg2+). Waste PET can be transformed into flakes, fibers, or aerogels. The adsorption capacity of such materials can be enhanced by grafting additional functional groups (e.g., chitosan) for the effective removal of Cr6+ ions from aqueous solutions with an efficiency of up to 110 mg mg−1.54 The alternative strategy is the copolymerisation of waste PET and PAA, followed by surface treatment with EDA to provide chelating properties. The fibers synthesised with a high content of ligand groups could remove Cu2+ and Ni2+ ions from water with an efficiency of up to 100 mg g−1.105 Waste PET can be upcycled to an aerogel coated with polydopamine (PD or PDA) to form a binder layer with polyethyleneimine. Consequently, a composite 3D aerogel bearing amino groups can effectively remove Pb2+, Hg2+ and Zn2+ ions from water with an efficiency of ∼96 mg g−1.56 The transesterification of PET with phenolic compounds46 exhibited enhanced adsorption of Cu2+ ions with 63 mg g−1 adsorption capacity in aqueous media compared to the waste PET. The removal efficiency of functional materials from PET waste is comparable to that of polymeric sorbents developed recently for eliminating heavy metal ions, with a sorption capacity of up to 100 mg g−1.141 The scope of contaminants has been broadened to dyes via surface aminolysis of PET bottles, after which the prepared PET hydrogel enables the removal of anionic Congo Red with an absorption capacity of ∼500 mg g−1.129
Upcycling of PET via formation of hybrid materials with surface MOFs is advantageous in terms of adsorptive application owing to the high surface area of the resulting composites. Our group demonstrated that PET@UiO-66 could remove imidacloprid with high adsorption capacity (4.5 mg g−1; Fig. 5a and Table 1(3)).41 Compared to mineral sorbents (1 mg g−1),142 PET@UiO-66 exhibited considerable technical advantages beyond the convenient sorbents: sustainable and affordable preparation, high adsorption capacity and rate, 100 times enhanced permeability compared to UiO-66 powder, and the possibility for application in fixed-bed columns. In addition, the previously developed material was used to deposit Ag NPs for removal and the photocatalytic degradation of paraoxon-ethyl (Table 1(3)).98
 Fig. 5  Application of functionalised PET waste: (a) removal of imidacloprid using PET@UiO-66, adapted with permission from ref. 41; (b) preparation of textile from waste PET by aminolysis, adapted from ref. 96, 99, 102 and 144; (c) comparison of functional upcycling of PET with mechanical and chemical strategies. *Qualitative analysis is based on limited examples of upcycling; **Concluded from basic considerations of analysis in Section S1 (ESI†). 
Finally, PET membranes, prepared by electrospinning of waste PET, followed by the hydrophobisation of the surface with PDMS or organic groups, can separate oil–water mixtures (Table 1(1)).89,91 These membranes were distinguished by high flux (∼20000 L m−2 h−1) and a high separation efficiency (>98%) for up to ten cycles, thereby displaying potential industrial applications.89
Enhancement of mechanical properties. 
The enhancement of thermomechanical properties of waste PET by compounding with low-cost waste validates their potential as ideal candidates for pavement engineering applications.103 Yang et al. showed how PET aggregates were treated using three different types of oxidizing agents such as H2O, H2O2, and Ca(ClO)2 for improved mechanical properties of concrete with coarse aggregates.143 Alternatively, the PET granules functionalised with butyl amine displayed improved flowability, setting period, and compressive strength of the cement paste to produce a value-added material.128 Functional upcycling alleviates the issues related to landfills, such as waste PET plastics, and converts these wastes into new value-added materials for constructing durable pavements.
Textile application. 
PET is widely used in the textile industry owing to excellent washability, wearability, antiwrinkle characteristics, and processability.83 Its replacement with upcycled materials is growing, as these are more affordable and display the same chemical structure as the virgin PET fibers. The fundamental experimental strategy is based on surface aminolysis, followed by other chemical transformations. The attachment of D-glucose,102 hydroxypropyltrimethyl ammonium chloride,96 nanocrystalline cellulose144 or ethylenediamine99 makes the PET surface more hydrophilic with enhanced hygroscopic properties (Fig. 5b). Consequently, the coloration of such upcycled fabric can be easily achieved – the fabric is economically dyed in a single bath and single stage. Moreover, the upcycled PET fabric is capable of withstanding multiple washing cycles.
Energy-related application. 
Waste PET bottles can be upcycled into triboelectric nanogenerators (TENGs) for energy harvesting by fabricating nanofiber-based aerogel using electrospinning and surface-assisted polymerisation. The obtained peak voltage (67.7 V) and current (9.4 μA) as performance characteristics are higher than those reported in several recent studies based on porous films or aerogels.145 Other energy-related devices include supercapacitors prepared from virgin PET.127 Interestingly, this strategy can be readily transferred to waste PET fabric. Barakzehi et al. grew MIL-53(Al) MOFs on PET fabric (Yazdbaf Co textile), followed by the immobilisation of graphene oxide for subsequent reduction and pyrrole polymerisation.127 This material serves as a flexible symmetrical solid-state supercapacitor with a capacitance volume of 510 mF cm−2.


2.1.3. Conclusions. 
PET is one of the most extensively studied polymers in the re-/upcycling industry compared to others. Therefore, mechanical (Section S1.1 in ESI†) and chemical (Section S1.2 in ESI†) recycling processes have remained highly diversified. A list of companies (e.g., Unilever, TakaTaka Solutions, and Beier) are already performing mechanical recycling on an industrial scale with a short time period and low cost (Fig. 5c). Although the mechanical recycling strategy has demonstrated undeniable success, chemical methods are actively developing and depolymerisation has been realised under various conditions. In general, chemical recycling of PET can provide valuable feedstock for the synthesis of various chemicals (Fig. 5c) including MOFs. However, this technique encounters several limitations such as high energy cost, undesirable product formation, and low-quality feedstocks. In search for more environmentally friendly conditions, new depolymerisation methods have been developed in the last five years, namely, electrochemical and photocatalytic processes (Section S1.2 in ESI†). Although these processes permit depolymerisation at room temperature, their selectivity is still underdeveloped. Therefore, the actual progress of chemical recycling is substantially high, and developed technologies are awaiting transfer to an industrial scale.
For diversification, certain chemical re-/upcycling methods have been transferred to functional upcycling for obtaining functional materials with an added value (Fig. 5c). The functional upcycling of PET waste represents a new sustainable and technological solution for waste PET processing and is promising for the development of new functional materials with desired properties and a diverse range of applications (e.g., antibacterial materials, adsorbents, and composites with enhanced adsorption properties). A special place is occupied by upcycling to PET coated with MOF-layers, because these porous compounds significantly improve the functionality and allow PET upcycling products to compete in the market. Thus, despite the significant achievements, the broad application field of PET makes it advisable to develop new functionalisation approaches for functional upcycling to incorporate a wide toolbox of organic functional groups in a controlled manner.

2.2. Polyethylene (PE)

2.2.1. General remarks. 
PE is the most consumed polymer worldwide and present approximately 35% of the total plastic waste streams with an annual global consumption of 104.4 Mt.71,146 Depending on the production process and consequent chemical structure and properties, PE can be segmented into three types: high-density polyethylene (HDPE) – linear; low-density polyethylene (LDPE) – branches containing both short- and long-chain branches; and linear low-density – linear low-density polyethylene (LLDPE) with mostly short-chain branches.
Based on the data analysis presented in the Eunomia report for Europe in 2018, an estimated 80% of rigid HDPE sold in the market ends up as waste, and from this waste, up to 41% is sorted and recycled.147 In 2018, the postconsumer HDPE output in Europe was estimated to supply 8% of the total European converter demand for HDPE resin. As estimated, approximately 62% of household and commercial PE flexible films (LDPE and LLDPE) was collected in Europe, among which ∼40% films were acquired from commercial streams with a recycling rate of ∼20%.148 Thus, PE is one of the most recyclable polymers. Nevertheless, the recycling methods are still limited to simple physical treatments.
PE waste polymers were found to be close to the virgin PE after aging.51 The variations in the structure, such as degradation and oxidization with the formation of carboxyl/hydroxyl groups, were observed only after exposure to harsh environmental conditions (e.g., sunlight, heat, oxidants, air, and humidity).149 In terms of bulk properties, PE loses its plasticity and transitions into an entirely brittle mechanical behaviour.150 The oxidative variations in the waste polymer structure can influence the reactivity of PE waste for re-/upcycling, especially adsorption.149,151 The commonly added stabiliser for PE are phenolic and amine antioxidants, hydrophobic organic chemicals, and polybrominated diphenyl ethers.152,153 Although the content level of PE stabilisers is low and does not exceed 1%, they can significantly influence the reactivity of certain catalytic systems used for re-/upcycling.154
Based on the corresponding reviews,20,155,156 the common mechanical (Section S2.1 in ESI†) and chemical re-/upcycling (Section S2.2 in ESI†) is discussed in Section S2 (ESI†). Accordingly, we conducted a comparative analysis of the benefits and limitations of functional upcycling with those of other PE recycling methods.


2.2.2. Functional upcycling. 
2.2.2.1. Structure of the polymer, active groups, and functionalisation agents. 
PE comprises long chains of ethylene (–CH2–CH2–) subunits produced by ethylene polymerisation (Fig. 6). The chemical structure of the polymer evidently limits the application of mild methods for its surface functionalisation owing to the inertness of C–H bonds. Thus, until 2013, only a few modification approaches were explored157 and further developments in the search of rational pathways for PE re-/upcycling yielded the design of novel approaches for PE functionalisation.
 Fig. 6  Functionalisation of PE: (a) radical grafting of polymers;158,159 (b) oxidation;160,161 (c) grafting in presence of organic peroxides and maleic anhydride (MAH);162 (d) amination;163–165 (e) thermal xanthylation;166 (f) halogenation;167 and (g) Ni, Rh organometallic catalysis.168–170 
The simplest approach for the functionalisation of waste PE-based materials is through the sorption of active components via noncovalent bonding such as layer-by-layer assembly,161,171 spin coating,172–174 or entrapment of active components by swelling in appropriate solvents, such as supercritical CO2.175
Physical treatment has been used for generating radicals on the PE surfaces. Generally, such methods have been extensively used for the surface polymerisation and preparation of graft polymers on the PE surface. For instance, application of γ-irradiation on PE substrates in the presence of monomers can realise radiation-induced grafting (co)polymerisation of N-vinyl-2-pyrrolidone,158N,N-dimethylamino ethyl methacrylate,176 vinyl trimethoxysilane,159 glycidyl methacrylate (GMA),177,178 and allylamine179 (Fig. 6a and Table 2(3)). Alternatively, PE/PP fabric can be activated by electron beam-induced180 GMA polymerisation and subsequent coupling with N-dimethyl-D-glucamine, triethylamine, and alkylation. The same monomer GMA was attached to PE/PP fibers by plasma-initiated grafting (Fig. 6a and Table 2(4)).181 Both types of polymer activation produced the design of new materials for environmental applications.

The physical methods of surface activation have been widely applied for the covalent grafting of small molecules or oxidation of the polymer backbone. The most common activation techniques include γ-ray irradiation, plasma treatment (oxygen, ammonia, and SO2 + H2O),171 or corona treatment (UV light + air)160,182 (Fig. 6b and Table 2(5)). After interaction with water or air, the formed hydroxyl or carboxyl groups can be involved in secondary transformations through allyl modification, N-ethyl-N′-(3-(dimethylamino)propyl)carbodiimide/N-hydroxysuccinimide (EDC/NHS) coupling, silanization, etc.
Furthermore, the chemical activation of the C–H bond is possible. The covalent modification of the PE backbone is similar to that of PP and involves oxidation or interaction with radical and radicaloid species. For instance, waste PE can be converted to covalent adaptable networks (CANs) via reactive extrusion in the presence of organic peroxides and MAH (Fig. 6c and Table 2(2)).162 In addition, benzoyl peroxide has been applied for the graft polymerisation of AA and dimethyl diallyl ammonium chloride (DMDAAC) directly on the PE surface (Fig. 6a and Table 2(1)).51
The radical transformation of C–H bonds has been conducted for grafting small molecules and functional groups. Similar to PP, N-centered radicals and nitrenes derived from diazo compounds have been successfully grafted on the PE surface (Fig. 6d).163–165 Moreover, sulphur-containing groups have been introduced by xanthylation (formation of C–S bond) under blue-light irradiation (Fig. 6e).166 Radical halogenation of PE has been realised for introducing reactive halogens (Fig. 6f).167,183–185
Not less important, PE can be functionalised via oxidation of C–C and C–H bonds under various conditions to form hydroxyl or carboxyl groups. Thus, oxidation has been performed by treatment with nitric186 or chromic acid,187 potassium permanganate, potassium persulfate, benzoyl peroxide188 or ozone161,189,190 (Fig. 6b and Table 2(5)). Recently, milder conditions have been employed for oxidation, wherein benzaldehyde and O2 were used for aerobic auto-oxidation by generating reactive radical intermediates for both C–H activation and exclusive carbonyl formation on PE (Fig. 6b).191
Note that functional upcycling methods involve the best practice from classic synthetic procedures including the utilisation of transition metal-based catalysts for transforming PE chains. The Hartwig group has contributed significantly to this area by utilising Ni, Rh, or Ru catalysts for directly introducing reactive groups such as hydroxyl, amino, borane or carbonyl groups to the PE chain (Fig. 6g).168–170
Overall, the methods applied for characterizing modified PE are similar to those used for other polyolefins. However, the solubility of PE is limited, which sufficiently restricts the application of solution-based techniques. In the case of post-polymerisation functionalisation of PE chains, NMR and gel permeation chromatography (GPC) are used to characterize the changes in the molecular weight distribution and chemical structure of a polymer, while XPS and FTIR continue to be the fundamental methods for studying surface properties and chemical composition.
2.2.2.2. Application of functional materials and comparison with previously reported materials. 
Mechanical and chemical recycling (Sections S2.1 and S2.2 in ESI†) of PE waste is a widespread approach, even though, recently, the research focus has started shifting toward functional upcycling. The most extensively studied application field is superwetting or adsorbent materials. For example, waste PE films have been employed to develop a novel amphoteric superabsorbent resin through polymerisation with AA and DMDAAC for water adsorption (Fig. 7a and Table 2(1)).51 The prepared material is a promising superwetting gel for agriculture, horticulture, and wastewater treatment. Superabsorbent hydrogels have been synthesised via polymerisation of waste PE with PAA (Table 2(1)).61 Resulting polymer have comparable water absorbency to that of newly prepared acrylic materials.200
 Fig. 7  Application of functionalised PE waste: (a) for water sorption, adapted from;51 (b) comparison of functional upcycling of PE with mechanical and chemical strategies. *Qualitative analysis based on limited examples of upcycling; **Concluded from basic considerations from analysis in Section S2, ESI.† 
Another idea, suggested by Garofalo et al., is to upgrade the mechanical properties of PE bags (with PP traces) by applying a nanotechnology-based strategy. In particular, various amounts of copolyamide or MAH were attached using a reactive extrusion process, and the developed functionalisation strategy enhanced the stiffness and ductility of PE (Table 2(2)).192,201
Despite the limited number of examples of PE functional upcycling, several studies have utilised commercially available PE to fabricate functional materials. Herein, we overview these examples to inspire scientists for implementing these strategies for upcycling of waste PE to produce new functional materials. Based on commercial PE materials, several functionalisation approaches have been applied, highlighting the adsorption applications of metal ions such as Au3+, Cu2+ (Table 2(3)),176,193 (Table 2(4)),181 boron (Table 2(3)),194 iodine (Table 2(3)),158 CO2 (Table 2(3)),180 and organic pollutants (pesticides and antibiotics) (Table 2(5)).149,199,202,203 The most common substrates include PE–PP nonwoven fibers or fabrics and films.
Across all strategies of PE functionalisation, numerous studies have been dedicated for designing antimicrobial, antifouling, and antioxidant surfaces for biomedical applications.204 The modern regulatory acts for medical devices (ISO 10993)205 do not regulate the source of plastic. However, additional sterilization and decontamination is required prior to the upcycling process affecting the mechanical and surface properties of PE and the additives released from PE.206–208 Potentially, functional upcycling of waste PE could be implemented in limited cases such as for designing noninvasive devices for skin or mucosal use (UPS class I and II); however it is essential to validate the biocompatibility of the upcycled materials according to the ISO 10993 and USP procedures. Certain strategies for modifying PE could be employed for functional upcycling via fabrication of noninvasive devices owing to the antifouling and antibacterial properties (cyclodextrins and polymers bearing phosphorylcholine groups) (Table 2(3)),178,195 antibacterial properties (polymers + antibiotic, quaternary ammonium salts, and metal NPs) (Table 2(5, 6)),190,209,210 and improved wettability and adhesion properties (allylamine) (Table 2(4)).196
As discussed, PE is the major polymer used for food packaging, where active elements could remove/preserve the growth of undesired molecules causing spoilage.211,212 To design functional materials for active food packaging, upcycled PE must conform to the specific regulations on plastic materials and objects intended to be in contact with food items. EC 10/2011 regulates the content of monomers and additives in plastic and overall migration limits.213 Currently, various desired functionalities are introduced in commercial PE, such as antimicrobial properties (chitosan, chitosan + pullulan, quaternary ammonium salts, and metal-fumed silica) (Table 2(5)),161,190,198 antioxidant activity (chitosan),171 ethylene scavenging behavior214 (halloysite nanotubes), oxygen scavenging behavior (pyrogallol, zero valent iron particles, and gallic-acid).215–217 We believe that these methods will be successfully implemented for functional upcycling of waste PE.
The wide electronic application of PE composites is related to lithium-ion batteries, where porous polyolefin films composed of PE are used as separators owing to their electrochemical stability, inertness, and mechanical strength. Various functionalisation strategies of commercially available PE separators can be implemented to improve heat stability and interfacial compatibility (e.g., phenolic resin and vinyl trimethoxysilane) (Table 2(3, 5)).159,197 The functionalisation of raw and commercially used PE for lithium-ion separator application has been reviewed,174,218 and the reported approaches on raw PE can be implemented for waste and commercially used PE materials.
Hartwig et al. have reported on the improvement of adhesiveness of PE toward metal and dyes without affecting their thermal stability or mechanical strength.170 The effect has been achieved by applying selective catalytic oxidation of PE.169,170 Furthermore, the authors developed a facile approach towards PE hydroxylation for enhancing the wetting properties and using the obtained product for graft polymerisation of lactones.169 The improvement of mechanical and rheological properties was achieved by Zhu et al. using a large-scale one-pot method for recycling PE into CANs, which can undergo dynamic exchange in response to external stimuli and can be used in the biomedical field and shape memory.219 The prepared PE materials exhibited clearly improved mechanical (e.g., tensile strength, Young modulus, and yield strength) and rheological (e.g., creep resistance) properties at elevated temperatures. Importantly, the obtained materials can be readily reprocessed, recycled, and 3D-printed to create opportunities for sustainable and high-performance PE materials.


2.2.3. Conclusions. 
As the most consumed polymer worldwide, PE is highly desired to be recycled. Both chemical and mechanical methods of PE recycling are thoroughly investigated (Fig. 7b and Section S2 in ESI†). Unlike other plastics, PE is often recycled with the same function: canisters become new canisters and films are regenerated into new films; however, the added value through such recycling is questionable. The added value of mechanically recycled products (Section S2.1 in ESI†) is extremely low, and therefore, the low price associated with mechanical recycling does not render this strategy reasonable for the future. In comparison, the chemical recycling of PE (Section S2.2 in ESI†) to valuable products such as dicarboxylic acids or gaseous and liquid hydrocarbons is considered promising and most feasible for industrial implementation in the near future (Fig. 7b). Although the high pyrolysis temperature and the release of greenhouse gases limit its industrial implementation, the chemical recycling strategy is widely promoted. Nonetheless, the prolongation of lifespan renders the functional upcycling of PE highly desirable in terms of circular economy as it offers a higher added value to the final materials. However, the inertness of the C–C bond limits the functional upcycling of PE and necessitates harsh and costly methods for designing high-valuable materials. Thus, only a few research studies have focused on the functional upcycling of waste PE. Thus, the functional upcycling of PE is highly desired, and further developments are required in large-scale functionalisation for competitiveness.

2.3. Polyvinyl chloride (PVC)

2.3.1. General remarks. 
PVC is a multifunctional, low-cost polymer that ranks 3rd in terms of global plastic production after PE and PP.220 PVC is distinguished from other polymers by strength, flame resistance, stiffness, durability, workability, and relatively low cost. Annually, nearly 50 Mt of PVC is produced at an annual increasing rate of ∼4%.221–224 Most manufactured PVC is widely applied in building and construction, electrical wire insulation, and disposable medical devices, with minor applications in packaging, clothing, leisure, sports, and other industries.1,225–227 Globally, most PVC waste is found in stores and landfills, and till 2008, only 0.2 Mt of PVC was recycled using mechanical and chemical pathways.228,229 By 2017, European Vinyl-Plus recycled 0.64 Mt of PVC, which will reach 1 Mt by 2030.230 Nevertheless, the major challenge of using PVC is the presence of chlorine in its structure, which can evolve during landfilling or incineration. Another critical challenge is the impossibility to incinerate PVC with other types of plastic because of (i) the required elevated temperature ≥1100 °C, and (ii) the reduced recycling ratio of plastics via deterioration of the structure.231,232
The chemical structure of PVC is modified during utilisation233 due to environmental exposure to sunlight, heat, moisture, oxygen, pollutants, mechanical stresses, and biological growth.234 The chemical variations often occur on the surface layer and include autoxidation, HCl release, peroxidation, chain scission, cross-linking, etc.233,235,236 Most PVC materials intensively absorb sunlight and actively undergo photolysis and photooxidation. The oxidation aging can hinder nucleophilic substitution or other reactions for re-/upcycling owing to the generation of oxygen-containing groups.228,235,237 The addition of thermal stabilisers could prevent the photodegradation of PVC.232 To accomplish application diversity, PVC contains multiple additives such as lubricants, impact-modifiers, flame retardants, reinforcing agents, stabilisers, fillers, colorants, antioxidants and plasticisers, which can be released during aging and affect further recycling processes20,230,238,239 through the possible side-reactions or poisoning of catalysts.20,154 Furthermore, the upcycling of aged PVC waste can create toxicity-related issues and release phthalate plasticisers, which are restricted because of their toxic effects.20,206,230,240
Through various recycling strategies, mechanical recycling is hindered by the release of Cl-containing chemicals, as detailed in Section S3.1 (ESI†) and the following reviews.20,241 The alternative chemical re-/upcycling approach is summarised in Section S3.2 (ESI†) and in certain previous studies.40,226,242–244


2.3.2. Functional upcycling. 
2.3.2.1. Structure of the polymer, active groups, and functionalisation agents. 
PVC is a polymer chain comprising (–CH2–CHCl–) units produced from vinyl chloride (Fig. 8). The presence of chlorine, the major limiting factor for chemical re-/upcycling, serves as the key advantage for functional upcycling owing to its ability to interact with a wide range of nucleophiles. In general, PVC functionalisation is performed using two principal approaches: (i) post-polymerisation modification process consuming the reactants in the dissolved state or melt, and (ii) surface modification, where the bulk material does not participate in the reaction.
 Fig. 8  Functionalisation of PVC: (a) azidation;44 (b) amination;245 (c) UV-irradiation or oxidation;246,247 (d) radical on-surface polymerisation;248 (e) cyanation/thiocyanation;44,249 (f) thiolation,250 and (g) elimination/hydroxylation.225,228–231 
Post-polymerisation modification is commonly based on nucleophilic substitution (SN2), where Cl is exchanged with various nucleophiles (e.g., S, N, O, and I), thereby modifying the polymer backbone.251,252 Such methods require preliminary crushing or powdering of waste PVC to obtain the necessary shape granules for enhancing the dissolution rate or melting. Thus, the surface modification approaches are more reliable for recycling PVC.
PVC surface modification is represented by a range of physical and chemical methods such as plasma253 and flame treatment,254 UV-irradiation,246 gamma rays,248,255–257 corona258 and glow247 discharge, atomic layer or vapor deposition,259,260 grafting,261 and nucleophilic substitution on the surface of the material262 (Fig. 8 and Table 3). Certain modification methods have been developed to enhance the mechanical characteristics and improve the interfacial properties of PVC surface for various applications, including antibiofouling materials.227 These methods have been tested primarily on commercially available PVC sources such as medical grade PVC, sheets and tubes, and urinary catheters. Nonetheless, only a handful of reports have been published on PVC waste functionalisation.254

Among all strategies, SN2 is the most effective approach for creating functional materials because of its economic advantages over other methods. The most common nucleophilic reagents are azides (Fig. 8a and Table 3(2)),44,263–265 thiols (Fig. 8f and Table 3(1)),227,250,266–268 amines (Fig. 8b and Table 3(6)),269–272 and less frequently, –CN or –SCN modification (Fig. 8e and Table 3(3)).44,249,273 These approaches were implemented on synthesised or commercial powder PVC and its commercial sheets and tubes. In certain instances, the reaction with nucleophiles leads to a relatively low conversion of functional groups, which can be improved by the addition of phase-transfer catalysts.249
Azide- or amine-terminated PVC can be implemented as a platform for subsequent functionalisation.263,274,275 PVC–N3 was mostly transformed via Cu-catalysed azide–alkyne cycloaddition (CuAAC ‘click chemistry’) for the bulk PVC276 or on its surface.44,277–279 The copper-free ‘click’ reaction was also reported.280 As an alternative to classic 'click' chemistry approaches, e.g., sulfur fluoride exchange (SuFEx), one-step reaction was used for introducing thiols, antibacterial agents, and ionic liquids by tethering benzophenone groups to the PVC surface.281 The general chemical modification methods of PVC have been previously overviewed in detail.252 Although such 'click' methods have been generally implemented with the Sigma-Aldrich PVC, these methods can be applied on various devices.44
Recent studies have reported the dechlorination process of PVC, wherein the reaction proceeds through parallel elimination (E2) and SN2.225,228 The technological approach follows the interaction with sources of –OH as NaOH228 or water225 (Fig. 8g and Table 3(7)). The degree of dechlorination increased up to nearly 100% at 300 °C in water225 and at 190 °C in NaOH.228 Although authors postulated that this process can be envisioned as a promising method for treating PVC waste, they did not mention sources of PVC waste in their studies.
2.3.2.2. Application of functional materials and comparison with previously reported materials. 
Despite the diverse toolbox of functional upcycling methods, no evidence has been reported for PVC waste processing. Considering the reactivity of PVC and available transformations (discussed in Section S2.3.2.1), PVC waste can be involved in functional upcycling. Therefore, we consider the examples of the functionalisation of commercially available PVC for functional materials (Table 3). The discussion of these reports can capture the interest of researchers for using PVC waste to produce new functional materials.
Generally, functionalised PVC has been used to design new materials for removing toxicants, separation membranes, catalysts, electrochemical and antimicrobial applications267,270,283,286,287 (Fig. 9a). The preparation of sorbents commonly requires a post-polymerisation modification of PVC to increase the loading of functional groups. For instance, Ganin et al. reported a method for post-polymerisation modification PVC amination to form composites with mesoporous silica (Fig. 9b and Table 3(6)).270 This material possessed a high surface area for CO2 capture and enhanced hydrophobicity for humid environment application. This technology enables the utilisation of waste PVC instead of a commercial PVC, which is highly promising. Jin et al. demonstrated that upcycled PVC can be employed to convert hydrogen carbonate (as a CO2 model source) to formate under hydrothermal conditions with 16% yield and 100% selectivity. The proposed strategy represents a new green approach for CO2 conversion simultaneously with PVC waste upcycling (Table 3(7)).225
 Fig. 9  Application of functionalised PVC waste: (a) antimicrobial application – modification of flexible PVC by nucleophilic substitution and subsequent reactions, adapted from ref. 267; (b) CO2 capture – amination of PVC by EDA, adapted from ref. 270; (c) comparison of functional upcycling of PVC with mechanical and chemical strategies. *Qualitative analysis based on limited examples of upcycling; **Concluded from basic considerations from analysis in Section S3 (ESI†); ***Reported only in commercial polymers. 
Yun et al. reported an example of amination, where PVC–polyethyleneimine (PEI) fibers were fabricated by dissolving both polymers, followed by the partial substitution of chlorine with the amino groups of PEI (Table 3(6)).245 The fibers were successfully employed for Pt4+ ion extraction with higher efficiency than commercial sorbents.
Although PVC acts as an insulator, functional upcycling can produce materials with unusual application in electrochemistry. Liu et al. enhanced the proton reduction activity of diiron complexes through covalent grafting to the PVC backbone using 'click' reaction with preliminary azidation (Table 3(2)).283
PVC plastics are nearly ideal materials for designing membranes due to their stability in acid or base media, enhanced mechanical properties, and availability.288 Currently, PVC-based membranes are widely used as proton exchange membranes, contaminant separators, and battery separators.286,289–294 Chemical modification of membranes commonly occurs through the formation of C–N and C–S bonds on the PVC surface. The attached functional group determines further application: L-cystein enables As5+ ion capture (Table 3(1)),282 sulfonated EDA was applied for proton-conductive membranes (Table 3(6)),284 polyamide and graphene oxide-polyamide for desalination materials (Table 3(6)),274,285 and trimethylamine improves membrane permeability and rejection with suitable antibacterial properties.295
Currently, commercial PVC is actively involved in the functionalisation cycle for further bio-related applications. Although waste PVC cannot be applied for catheter fabrication due to the strict regulations on medical plastics operating in contact with the human body,205 it can be applied in alternative fields. For instance, after sterilization and appropriate regulations, PVC can be upcycled to introduce bactericidal or antifouling properties for coatings or the creation of antibacterial surfaces. Upcycled PVC can be used as containers for storage205 because of the ability to reduce leaching additives.280,296 In particular, treatment of PVC with γ-rays facilitates grafting of various polymers on the PVC surface to enhance the bactericidal (Table 3(4))255 and antifouling properties (Table 3(4)),257 while reducing bacterial adhesion (Table 3(4)).248 Furthermore, oxygen glow discharge reduces bacterial adhesion via the generation of –COOH and –OH groups (Table 3(5)).247 As such, nucleophilic substitution methods offer similar applications in the design of antifouling or antimicrobial PVC-based materials. In the near future, we anticipate the transfer of bactericidal/antifouling technologies to PVC waste. For example, the VinylplusMed project was launched in 2021 to recycle PVC-composed medical waste and promote the idea of accelerating sustainability in healthcare.297


2.3.3. Conclusions. 
Despite the variability in methods for modification and chemical recycling of PVC (Section S3.2 in ESI†), the current status of functional upcycling is far from its technological realization (Fig. 9c). Potentially, functional upcycling can overcome one of the key limitations, i.e., leaching of dangerous plasticisers from PVC into the environment.280,296,298 Moreover, the existing mechanical (Section S3.1 in ESI†) and chemical (Section S3.2 in ESI†) approaches do not correspond to the concept of circular economy and sustainable development in terms of carbon footprint, energy consumption, and contamination of surrounding media. Due to these reasons, functional upcycling should be the fundamental goal for further utilisation of PVC.

2.4. Polypropylene (PP)

2.4.1. General remarks. 
PP is the second most used polymer worldwide after PE and accounts for almost 20% of the total plastic volume, with 75 Mt per year in waste streams.71,299 The market volume was expected to reach almost 100 Mt by 2029. Based on the data analysis reported in Eunomia 2018 for Europe, only up to 28% of the rigid PP is sorted and transferred for reprocessing. The postconsumer PP output in Europe in 2018 was estimated to supply only 3% of the total European conversion demand of PP resin.148
The major application areas of PP include packaging and industrial fields because of its exceptional properties such as high resistance, moisture barrier, flexibility, toughness, high durability, and lightweight.300 Additionally, PP is used for surgical equipment, drugs, auto components, and cosmetics. COVID-19 has significantly affected PP consumption, as billions of single-use face masks (FMs) – composed of nonwoven PP fabric – are used worldwide on a daily basis.301 In India and USA, more than 3 kilotons of PP waste are produced from FM per week, and incinerating these masks is not ideal from the standpoints of circular economy and carbon footprint.302 Thus, the management and treatment of PP waste has emerged as a significant problem. For this reason, scientists are devising new eco-friendly technologies to manage this waste.
Compared with other polymers, PP is prone to aging and undergoes degradation and oxidization only under harsh environmental conditions to yield carboxyl/hydroxyl groups upon exposure to sunlight, heat, oxidants, air, and humidity,303 which consequently alters its reactivity.42 The aging of PP results in increased crystallinity, formation of surface cracks, and embrittlement.304 The added photostabiliser and thermal stabiliser can affect the reactivity of PP during re-/upcycling. The most common ones are phenolic antioxidants (radical scavenger) and phosphorus antioxidants (peroxide decomposition agents), used as thermal stabilisers and UV absorbers, and hindered amines are used as UV stabilisers.305 Therefore, the composition of PP waste should be evaluated before developing a relevant re-/upcycling strategy.
The previous achievements in the field of mechanical recycling (Section S4.1 in ESI†) are summarised in the following reviews20 (Section S4, ESI†). Although chemical recycling (Section S4.2 in ESI†) has been reviewed as a separate chapter in recent publications,155,156 we further compared the functional upcycling method with other methods of PP waste recycling based on these reviews.


2.4.2. Functional upcycling. 
2.4.2.1. Structure of the polymer, active groups, and functionalisation agents. 
PP is a typical polyalkane containing 2-methylethylene units (–CH2–CH2(–CH3)–) produced by propylene polymerisation (Fig. 10). Similar to chemical recycling (Section S4.2 in ESI†), the low reactivity of the C–H bonds requires harsh conditions or relatively high energy input for transformation. Nevertheless, most upcycling methods do not involve the functionalisation of the C–H bond, and instead, rely on relatively weak noncovalent interactions with the surface of PP.
 Fig. 10  Functionalisation of PP: (a) thermal xanthylation;306 (b) radical grafting of polymers;307–309 (c) heteroradicals functionalisation;167,310 (d) sulfonation;311 (e) grafting under physical treatment;312,313 (f) oxidation;42,314,315 (g) grafting of C-centered radicals;316 and (h) diazirine modification through carbene intermediate.317 
As discussed earlier, the widespread application of PP FMs make them the most common waste candidate for functional upcycling. Most upcycling approaches are based on noncovalent interactions such as dip-coating,49,52,318 electrospraying,319 soaking,50 drop-casting,320,321 sputtering,322 on-surface preparation of NPs323 and layer-by-layer assembly.324 The common approaches have been improved by applying supercritical CO2 as a solvent for impregnating the active components into surface layers.325 Additionally, laser-induced deposition of few-layer graphene-like structures on nonwoven FMs has been described.326,327
Despite the simplicity and convenience of noncovalent approaches, the leakage of active components to the surrounding media limits their widespread application, which can be resolved via covalent modification. Thus, the sulfonation of PP in concentrated sulfuric acid has been implemented to improve their thermal,328,329 adsorption performance330 or electrochemical properties311 (Fig. 10d and Table 4(2)).
