Another approach of surface functionalisation utilises the interaction of reactive radicals with C–H bonds. For instance, a composite material from MAH and PP and acrylonitrile-butadiene rubber (NBR)307 has been formed during the extrusion of polymer mixtures in the presence of dicumyl peroxide (DCP) at 180 °C (Fig. 10b and Table 4(3)).307,308 Some azodicarboxylates are also able to interact with the PP surface to generate active sites for further polymerisation of poly(ethylene glycol) methacrylate (Fig. 10b and Table 4(3)).309
An exciting approach has been developed for covalent grafting of a photosensitiser through treatment with a carbene source (diazirine) (Fig. 10h).317 To the best of our knowledge, this is a relatively more moderate method for the covalent functionalisation of PP surfaces. As reported, C–C bonds can be generated during thermal-assisted grafting of bis(phenylsulfonyl)ethylene in combination with photocatalysts (Fig. 10g).316
In addition to chemical treatment, the modification techniques based on physical approaches are widely applied because of their facile accessibility and versatility. Hydroxyl (O–H) groups on PP masks and meshes have been introduced by oxygen plasma treatment314 or UV-irradiation42 (Fig. 10f and Table 4(5)). The formation of active sites using high-energy sources such as γ-ray radiation,331,332 electron beam,313 UV333 and plasma312 allows for the covalently attachment of different organic molecules (e.g., acrylonitrile, polymethyl acrylate, PS, GMA, AA, and benzyl chloride) to the PP surface (Fig. 10e and Table 4(4)).
The discussion on possible routes for PP upcycling is not complete without the consideration of methods for modifying virgin polymers. Such pathways can be notably used for creating new materials from PP waste.
First, the modification procedures aim to oxidise the C–C backbone using catalytic (for instance, Rh-catalysed borylation followed by hydroxylation334) or stoichiometric regimes (potassium persulfate in water dispersion335). In addition, ozone treatment methods have been applied for surface hydroxylation (Fig. 10f and Table 4(5)).315
A variety of functionalisation approaches for PP are based on the interaction with radicals or active species, including the heteroatomic radicals (Fig. 10c). For instance, azido groups have been grafted on the PP surface via reaction with a hypervalent iodine reagent – azidoiodane. Subsequently, azido groups have been applied for azide–alkyne cycloaddition (Fig. 10c).310 Sulfur-containing reactive groups have been grafted through thermal-assisted xanthylation in the presence of peroxide (Fig. 10a).306 Furthermore, PP undergoes thermo- and light-induced radical halogenation using N-haloamides, which has been used to prepare fluorinated,336 chlorinated, and brominated PP (Fig. 10c).167
The methods employed for the characterization of composite and hybrid materials derived from PP are limited to spectroscopic techniques (XPS, FTIR, and energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS)). Nevertheless, the specific modification approaches presume the application of other methods for the characterization of immobilized materials (such as XRD for MOFs or elemental analysis).
2.4.2.2. Application of functional materials and comparison with previously reported materials. 
The enormous generation of PP waste after the start of COVID-19 pandemic has created additional challenges for plastic waste management. Owing to this reason, the majority of studies dedicated to the functional upcycling of PP waste focuses on the application of FM.
The hydrophobic nature of PP fibers enables the implementation of functionalised waste FMs for oil–water separation processes. Nevertheless, the achievement of high performance for separation requires additional functionalisation of the PP surface with hydrophobic components. To date, various techniques have been adopted including the formation of composite with superhydrophobic epoxy resin/SiO2 (Table 4(1)),52 immobilisation of superhydrophobic fluorine-free MAF-6 (Fig. 11a and Table 4(1)),50 or treatment with heptane (Table 4(5)).315 Upcycled PP waste can be used for water purification as well as for selective gas capture from various gas mixtures. Wang et al. reported the separation of propene and propane using ZIF-8 MOFs immobilised on PP membranes.337 Despite using commercially available membranes, such an approach provides a perspective for the functional upcycling of PP waste.
 Fig. 11  Application of functionalised PP waste: (a) for an oil–water separation process, reprinted with permission from ref. 50; copyright 2022, Elsevier, (b) enhanced mechanical properties, adapted from ref. 307; (c) comparison of functional upcycling of PP with mechanical and chemical strategies. *Qualitative analysis based on limited examples of upcycling; **Concluded from basic considerations from analysis in Section S4, ESI.† 
In addition to separation application, functional upcycling of PP can considerably enhance the sorption performance. In principle, the simple upcycling of FMs through UV-aging improves their adsorption capacity and promotes their application in removing metal ions from water (Table 4(4)).42 Using this strategy, Badyal et al. developed a multistage procedure for creating a composite material from PP fabric via pulsed plasma coating with poly(vinylbenzyl chloride), followed by covalent grafting of calixarene (Table 4(4)).312 The prepared membrane is able to selectively entrap Cr6+ oxoanions, making it attractive for point-of-use application in geographic regions lacking appropriate wastewater treatment plants or flawed environmental monitoring systems. Independently, PP fabric has been coated with electroconductive polyaniline (PANI) for adsorbing humic acid (Table 4(1)).49
PP-based materials are an attractive platform for the preparation of multitarget adsorbents. Thus, layer-by-layer assembly of polydopamine has been implemented for the immobilisation of layered double Ni–Al hydroxide films on PP chips for the adsorption of organic dyes and pharmaceuticals (Table 4(1)).324 Furthermore, enhanced vapor (NH3, CH2O, and H2O2) adsorption has been demonstrated on waste PP fibers loaded with ZnS, which is an even more challenging application (Table 4(1)).323 In addition to the removal of vapors, upcycled PP provides an interesting platform for vapor sensing. The functionalisation of PP waste with poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene)polystyrene sulfonate (PEDOT:PSS) and porphyrins enables the design of a sensor for ethanol, water, hexane, toluene, acetone, acetic acid, and triethylamine (Table 4(1)).320
To develop novel eco-friendly technologies for utilising waste FMs, scientists have been inspired to design electronic devices. For instance, the deposition of gold with a polypyrrole coating (Table 4(1)),322 and the use of Ca3Co4O9-δ perovskite and graphene ink (Table 4(1))321 have been demonstrated as methods to create novel materials for supercapacitors. Sulfonated PP is applied as a separator for aqueous rechargeable batteries and exhibits remarkable safety and electrochemical performance (Table 4(2)).311
In addition, several surface engineering methods have been applied for preparing composites with enhanced mechanical properties (tensile/elongation). The preparation of blends with NBR via additional cross-linking provides enhanced thermomechanical properties and improved tensile and barrier properties (Fig. 11b and Table 4(3)).307
Evidently, across all strategies of PP functionalisation, numerous studies have focused on fabricating antimicrobial and antiviral surfaces. Despite the variability of methods and promising achievements in this field, the applicability of such approaches to PP upcycling remains a challenge because of the actual impossibility of reusage of personal protective masks. Although several strategies are employed to reuse FM by decontamination or disinfection, these procedures reduce the efficiency of the FMs.338 To avoid overabundant citations, we refer to recent reviews dedicated to preparing PP-based fibers with improved properties.338–344


2.4.3. Conclusions. 
The abundance of PP in the current market necessitates re-/upcycling as a crucial step for further development in polymer utilisation. The chemical inertness of PP (similar to PE) limits the successful implementation of chemical processes requiring sufficient energy consumption or harsh reaction conditions (Section S4.2 in the ESI†). Nevertheless, chemical recycling is considered promising and the most feasible approach for industrial implementation in a short period (Section S4.2 in the ESI†), (Fig. 11c). However, the prolongation of lifespan increases the demand of functional upcycling of PP in terms of circular economy owing to the higher added value cost of the final materials. Although these developed approaches can partially resolve the problem of PP upcycling, this aspect requires further investigation. To date, a majority of methods for upcycling are associated with the sorption of functional components via relatively weak interactions, which increases the chances for leakage. Moreover, the fiber structure of FMs can be easily fragmented and release large quantities of PE nanoplastics and microplastics, causing significant health risks during re-/upcycling.345 Despite the emergence of covalent modification approaches, their application as industrial technologies is still far from realisation. Thus, further exploration of novel strategies for the selective functionalisation of PP surfaces will significantly impact future developments of PP upcycling to yield high added value products for the circular economy and maximization of PP lifespan (Fig. 11c).

2.5. Polystyrene (PS)

2.5.1. General remarks. 
PS accounts for approximately 10 wt% of the total plastic waste produced annually in the last ten years due to its high abundance as an industrial, packaging, and household polymer.71,346 By 2024, it is forecasted to grow to an expected production capacity of 15.68 Mt.347 The most common forms of PS include solid blocks, films, and expanded foams. Primarily, 30–50% of the plastic waste is associated with electronic equipment and energy production and transfer.348 The most important challenge in PS collection and recycling is related with low-density PS-based materials.20 Although the Alliance of Foam Packaging Recyclers has reported that the recycling rate for postconsumer and postcommercial PS in the United States has increased from 20% in 2008 to 28% in 2010,349 it is relatively less than that of most solid polymer wastes.
The structure of PS-based plastic waste can significantly differ from virgin PS because of aging during the utilisation cycle. PS undergoes degradation upon exposure to sunlight, heat, oxidants, air, and humidity.350 In terms of bulk properties, PS loses its mechanical and tensile properties under the effect of UV light and heat.350,351 As such, light induces the generation of radicals via oxidation, inducing cross-linking accompanied by chain scissions. The oxidative variations in the waste polymer structure can affect the reactivity of PS waste for re-/upcycling. To overcome or restrict this deterioration in reactivity, (photo)stabilisers are used to reduce unwanted interactions and ensure acceptable lifespan under outdoor exposure conditions. The most common applications include flame retardants, UV absorbers, excited-state quenchers, peroxide decomposers, and radical scavengers.352 Although the content of PS stabilisers does not exceed 5%, they can significantly affect the reactivity of certain catalytic systems used for re-/upcycling through chemical transformations.353
The most recent developments in recycling PS-based plastics are summarised in ref. 348 and 354. The common methods of mechanical20 (Section S5.1 in ESI†) and chemical re-/upcycling29,33,355 (Section S5.2 in ESI†) are briefly discussed in Section S5 (ESI†) based on the corresponding reviews and used for comparatively analysing the upcycling approaches.


2.5.2. Functional upcycling. 
2.5.2.1. Structure of the polymer, active groups, and functionalisation agents. 
PS is a long hydrocarbon chain with phenyl groups linked to alternating carbon atoms composed of styrene monomers obtained from liquid petrochemicals. The presence of relatively electron-rich phenyl rings facilitates the introduction of functional groups (e.g., sulfonic, acyl, alkyl, nitro, perfluoroalkyls, etc.) via common electrophilic substitution (Fig. 12a and c), offering ample opportunities for further chemical modification tailored to specific applications.
 Fig. 12  Functionalisation of PS: (a) nitration;48 (b) sulfonation using different sources of electrophilic;59,356–360 (c) Friedel–Crafts acylation/alkylation;361–363 (d) formation of hydrazones;364 (e) cross-linking reaction;365–367 (f) radical trifluoromethylation;368,369 (and g) hydroarylation.110 
The nitration of PS has been applied to fabricate materials with enhanced dielectric properties for energy storage applications.48 The reaction proceeds under moderate conditions using a mixture of sulfuric and nitric acids as nitrating agents (Fig. 12a and Table 5(1)).

PS sulfonylation is the most extensively studied reaction due to the simplicity of the chemical process (Fig. 12b and Table 5(2)).360,370 The most common and easy-to-use reagent for PS sulfonylation is sulfuric acid, which was first reported in 1999.371 Following studies investigated more moderate reaction conditions such as silica–sulfuric acid (Fig. 12b and Table 5(2))358 and acetyl sulfate (Fig. 12b and Table 5(2)).359 Additionally, electrostatic interactions between sulfonic groups and NH2 moieties of organosilanes are used to deposit PS-sulfonic on SiO2 using the sol–gel method (Fig. 12b).359
The second common reaction is the Friedel–Craft acylation/alkylation of PS waste catalysed by common Lewis acids (AlCl3 and FeCl3) or ZnO under microwave irradiation.355 Acylation of PS surfaces is performed using succinic or MAHs (Fig. 12c and Table 5(3)),361–363,372,373 acetyl chloride,364 and acetic anhydride.374 Moreover, acylation is often employed in the formation of reactive centers for grafting amines (e.g., dopamine, hydrazone, and tetraethylenepentamine) for the target application (Fig. 12d). The alkylation of PS can be used for cross-linking using additional reagents to prepare hyper-cross-linked polymers (HCPs) with high porosity and surface areas (Fig. 12e and Table 5(4)).375,376 The common external cross-linking reagents include formaldehyde366,377 or halogen-containing compounds such as dichloroalkane (dichloromethane and 1,2-dichloroethane),365 carbon tetrachloride,378 or α,α′-dichloro-p-xylene, and (4,4′-bis(chloromethyl)-1,1′-biphenyl).376,378
Leibfarth et al. pioneered the functionalisation of PS waste (Fig. 12f and Table 5(6)),368,369 with a fundamental idea of upcycling through nonselective trifluoromethylation (or fluoroalkynylation) of the PS backbone using photocatalytically-generated radical species from the available trifluoroacetic anhydride in the presence of Ru-based or organic photocatalysts. Currently, the proposed approach can be considered straightforward for preparing hydrophobic polymers from PS wastes. In addition to typical electrophilic or radical substitution, the PS surface is oxidised by relatively strong oxidants such as chromic acetic solution, which results in the formation of carboxylic groups for further electrostatic interaction with PEI (Table 5(5)).60
Similar to PET,46 Azoulay et al. demonstrated a gold-catalysed intermolecular hydroarylation between alkyne derivatives and PS, resulting in the direct functionalisation of phenyl rings through 1,2-substituted methyl acrylate (Fig. 12g and Table 5(7)).110 The solubility of PS and derivatives enables a wide range of techniques for characterizing the obtained materials. The bulk modification has been evaluated by NMR spectroscopy, GPC, or simple titration when acidic groups are introduced. For the surface modification of PS, common observational methods such as FTIR, XPS, and scanning electron microscopy (SEM) with EDS have been implemented to analyse the surface composition and properties.
2.5.2.2. Application of functional materials and comparison with previously reported materials. 
The functional upcycling of PS results in the generation of new high-performance materials. The most common class is high-adsorption capacity adsorbents for metal ions, dyes, drugs, CO2, and phenols (Fig. 13a and Table 5(3, 4, 5)).45,57,60,362–365 For the negatively charged surface caused by the presence of sulfo or carboxylic groups, various types of toxins and ions such as cationic toxins, metal ions (Table 5(3)),360,362,379,380 and cationic dyes (Table 5(3))45,363 can be successfully removed. Conversely, the positively charged amino groups present on the surface can facilitate the capture of anionic dyes45 or phenols via hydrogen bonding (Table 5(3)).364 In addition, the presence of relatively electron-rich aryls inducing dipole interactions with CO2 or CO2-philic amine groups is favorable for CO2 capture and utilisation in the separation of a N2/CH4 mixture.381 The adsorption capacity for metal ions on PS waste-derived adsorbents ranges from 5–65 mg g−1,362,379,380 which is less than or comparable to that of modern polymer composite adsorbents,382,383 but higher than that of cross-linked high-power field (HPF) membranes produced from PS and polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP),384 sulfonated fly ash,385 or magnetic carbon nanotube (CNT)-based aerogels.386
 Fig. 13  Application of functionalised PS waste: (a) modification of PS by 1,2,4-benzenetricarboxylic anhydride for adsorption of dyes, adapted from ref. 45; (b) sulfonation of PS waste for catalytic application in biomass valorization, adapted from ref. 359; and (c) comparison of functional upcycling of PS with mechanical and chemical strategies *Qualitative analysis based on limited examples of upcycling; **Concluded from basic considerations from analysis in Section S5, ESI.† 
As the adsorption capacity for dyes is significantly high (1000–2300 mg g−1),45,363 it generally outperforms the hyperbranched polymers387 and three-dimensional graphene-based macrostructures.388,389 The adsorption capacity of PS grafted with hydrazone for phenol is 178 mg g−1 (Table 5(3)),364 which is comparable or even superior to that of previously published ion-exchange resins (92 mg g−1)390 or molecular-imprinted polymers (86 mg g−1).391
The adsorption capacity for CO2 of hyper-cross-linked waste PS was 2–2.52 mmol g−1 at 1 bar (Table 5(4)).365,366,378 An increased capacity of 11 mmol g−1 was registered at 10 bar,367 which is comparable to that of activated carbon materials,392 zeolites, and certain MOFs.393,394 Ghaemi et al. demonstrated another application of HCP waste PS for O2/N2 separation with an adsorption capacity of 12.78 and 7.67 mmol g−1 for O2 and N2, respectively, at 5 °C and 10 bar. This material overperforms waste polycarbonate HCP, silica gel, and zeolite adsorbents in terms of adsorption capacity (Table 5(4)).377
An alternative application of sulfonated PS waste is its use as an acidic catalyst for transformation of readily available and affordable sugars into immensely significant furfuryl alcohols (Fig. 12b, 13b and Table 5(2))59,359,395 as well as in biodiesel synthesis (Fig. 13b),359 furfural oxidation to maleic and succinic acids, and cyclization of (+)-citronellal (Fig. 12b and Table 5(2)).357 Lim et al. demonstrated that PS food boxes can be easily converted into acidic homogeneous or cross-linked porous heterogenous polymeric catalysts under mild reaction conditions (Table 5(2)).59 Notably, the degree of sulfonation in waste-derived catalysts (∼30%) is less than that in the commercially available poly(4-styrenesulfonic acid). Nevertheless, the yields of converting 5-hydroxymethylfurfural were comparable under identical reaction conditions. Granados et al. reported the high technological performance of SiO2-attached sulfonated PS as a catalyst, distinguished by the ease of separation from the reaction mixture and reutilisation.359
The photocatalytic C–H fluoroalkylation of PS holds high potential for achieving high surface energy for superhydrophobic, anti-icing, and antifouling materials. An increase in the water contact angle from 94 to 111° is demonstrated on fluorinated PS films (Table 5(6)).368,369 Considering a functionalisation degree of 30 mol%, this approach holds great potential as a foundation for future applications in corrosion resistance, anti-icing, anti-fogging, anti-bacterial, and self-cleaning applications.
Finally, Chen et al. developed a process for PS waste foam acetylation using acetic anhydride to improve the quality of crude oil. In particular, the addition of the prepared material reduces the viscosity and the pour point of the crude oil by up to 84.6% and 8.8 °C, respectively.374


2.5.3. Conclusions. 
The development of straightforward approaches for re-/upcycling of PS remains a crucial issue for modern technology. Through various recycling strategies, it has been observed that mechanical recycling (Section S5.1 in ESI†) and landfills do not offer materials with a high added value (Fig. 13c). Although chemical recycling (Section S5.2 in ESI†) is presently gaining momentum, it still involves harsh reaction conditions for processing. Therefore, functional upcycling is a promising alternative for PS waste utilisation, which promotes the design of functional materials outperforming commercially available analogues. To advance in this direction, scientists have to design novel approaches for converting PS wastes into valuable materials with specific properties and applications.

2.6. Polyurethane (PU)

2.6.1. General remarks. 
PU is the sixth most used polymer worldwide (8% of produced plastics) with an annual production volume of 18 Mt.396 PU-based polymers are primarily classified into foams and in denominated coatings, adhesives, sealants, and elastomers (CASEs). PUs are petrochemical-based polymers composed of carbamate (urethane) units, where the structure of units predetermines the properties of the polymer. Nevertheless, the chemical structure of the polymer backbone causes serious hazards to the environment due to the potential formation of isocyanates, cyanic acid, and dioxins during degradation/recycling. The major application fields of PU include furniture (33%), construction (13%), electronics and appliances (11%), automobiles (10%), etc.1 Although PU generates a significant amount of waste, landfilling is the first choice for waste disposal in several countries accounting for ∼30.8% of the total waste volume.397,398 In turn, 39.5% is recovered through energy recovery processes and only 29.7% is recycled. Thus, the development of rational approaches for re-/upcycling of PU is a vital task for modern chemical engineering.
The functional upcycling of waste PU (WPU) should be performed considering the variations in structures and properties associated with aging. Depending on the applications, the performance degradation of PU can range from the deterioration of mechanical properties to the destruction of the chemical structure of the polymer. This is specifically important for PU exposed to air, under UV-irradiation, or under heating in a humid environment.399 The thermal degradation proceeds according to the radical chain mechanism at the α-position of the ether bridge. Additionally, the oxidation process – initiated by free radicals from hydroperoxides – results in the formation of carbonyl groups or chain scission.400 Thermal degradation generally includes the release of toxic gases. Accordingly, to avoid this degradation, ready-to-use polymers contain stabilisers such as flame retardants, antioxidants, UV absorbers, excited-state quenchers, peroxide decomposers, and radical scavengers.400,401 Although the content of PU stabilisers does not exceed 10%,400 they can significantly affect the reactivity of certain catalytic systems applied for chemical re-/upcycling. Thus, the development of functional upcycling techniques, which are independent of the presence of additives, represents a challenging task.
The earlier achievements of PU waste disposal are summarised in ref. 396, 402, and 403 (Section S6 in the ESI†). Herein, the functional upcycling approaches are compared with the mechanical and chemical re-/upcycling methods based on its description given in Section S6 (Sections S6.1 and S6.2 in ESI†).


2.6.2. Functional upcycling. 
2.6.2.1. Structure of the polymer, active groups, and functionalisation agents. 
As discussed earlier, PU is a polycondensation product containing carbamate units (Fig. 14). In principle, the fundamental pathways for modification should include a wide range of surface transformations via the partial cleavage of C–O or C–N bonds through surface hydrolysis, glycolysis, and aminolysis.404 However, the chemical transformations of the PU (Section S6.2 in ESI†) surface differ from the chemical upcycling methods (Section S6.2 in ESI†). Although the fundamental aim of chemical recycling is to cleave the PU backbone, the PU surface chemistry primarily seeks to attach the functional groups.
 Fig. 14  Functionalisation of PU: (a) on-surface radical polymerisation;405,406 (b) surface hydrolysis followed by diazotization and secondary transformations;407–409 (c) functionalisation with diphenyl diisocyanate followed by silanisation;410 and (d) diazonium surface functionalisation for surface hydrophobization.411,412 
One of the most applicable methods for functional upcycling of PU is the immobilisation of various active groups (including polymeric ones) on its surface. PU has a perfect adhesive property towards a wide range of polymers. For instance, PANI NPs can be immobilised on the PU surface via oxidative polymerisation (Table 6(3)).413 A similar route was adopted for the immobilisation of PD using self-polymerisation on both waste and commercial PU under normal conditions (Fig. 14a and Table 6(3));414 however, the polymer layer was not attached covalently. In addition to polycondensation, free radical polymerisation can be used to create composite polymeric materials on the PU surface. Thus, lauryl methacrylate and/or hexadecane (HD) were copolymerised in the presence of cross-linkers and benzoyl peroxide as an initiator for the surface modification of waste PU (Table 6(1)).405 Polymerisation on the PU surface has emerged as a common strategy for functional upcycling.414–416

The other covalent modification techniques are represented by the surface hydrolysis of the PU treated with a hydrochloric acid solution under reflux for 2 h (Fig. 14b).407 The formed free amino groups in conserved PU foams can undergo diazotization, azo coupling, and other reactions typical for aromatic amines.408 For instance, Páez-Hernández et al. demonstrated PU diazotization followed by coupling with O-ethyl dithiocarbonate (Fig. 14b and Table 6(2)).409 Alternatively, PU was treated with 4,4′-methylene diphenyl diisocyanate to introduce reactive –NCO groups, which provides an excellent structural basis for surface modification with hexadecyltrimethoxysilane using triethanolamine as a catalyst (Fig. 14c and Table 6(4)).410
More recently, electrostatic interactions have been used to recycle waste PU and transform it into functional material. SiO2 NPs have been deposited on PU by dipping (Table 6(3))417via electrostatic interactions and hydrogen bonding.418 Furthermore, this material was coated with octadecyltrimethylsiloxane for oil–water separation. Alternatively, waste PU foam has been used as a substrate for a supercapacitor (Table 6(3))47 formed by deposition of CNTs and cellulose nanofiber (CNF) suspension to interact with PU for further surface chemical transformations.47,419,420
Although the functional upcycling of PU is gaining momentum, the chemistry of commercially available PU products is quite diverse. Therefore, we will explore further examples of virgin PU functionalisation considering its potential applicability to waste PU. Ellison and Dichtel used dibutyltin dilaurate as a cross-linking agent for PU foams/films to enable their reprocessing at elevated temperatures via dynamic carbamate exchange reactions. Commercial cross-linked PU foams were continuously reprocessed using twin-screw extrusion to remove the gaseous filler and produce PU filaments or films with elastomeric or rigid thermoset mechanical properties.55
Only a few examples of diazonium modification of PU are reported, while the versatility of substituents in diazonium salts enables the covalent attachment with fluorinated or amino functional groups (Fig. 14d and Table 6(4)).406,411,412 These groups can provide additional functionality to PU or used in advanced surface chemistry.
The influence of oxidation in PU waste functionalisation is another reaction that requires further investigation. The surface of commercially available PU stents was first oxidised by the Jones reagent (CrO3 + H2SO4) to form a –COOH group to promote EDC/NHC coupling with chitosan fatty acids (stearic acid, oleic acid, and linoleic acid) to prevent bacterial colonization (Fig. 14b and Table 6(2)).415 Potentially, recycled polyols can be grafted to waste PU, providing functional materials from both waste components.421 Chun reported a unique and promising upcycling approach in which recycled polyols from waste PU foams (prepared by thermal depolymerisation at 200 °C) were grafted onto PU to improve its properties, e.g., tensile strength, shape recovery, low-temperature flexibility, and water compatibility.421
Several available methods such as XPS, FTIR, or EDS can be used to characterize the surface functionalisation of the PU surface.


2.6.2.2. Application of functional materials and comparison with previously reported materials. 
The functional upcycling of PU leads to the generation of new high-performance materials, especially high-capacity adsorbents for metal ions or oil–water separation. A common strategy for selective oil removal is hydrophobization of the surface using poly(lauryl methacrylate), polyhexadecene, or long alkyl chains (Tables 6(1, 3)).405,417 The adsorption capacity of upcycled sponges is comparable to that of sponges prepared from virgin PU (Table 6(4)).411,412 Conversely, PU has been functionalised using PD and Fe(OH)3 to hydrophilize hydrophobic PU (Table 6(3)).414 Owing to the superhydrophilicity, water can be immediately trapped in the hierarchical surface structure of Fe(OH)3@PDA@WPU.
To remove toxic Hg2+ ions, the PU surfaces were grafted with PANI (Table 6(3))413 or O-ethyl dithiocarbonate (Table 6(2))409 to achieve a removal efficiency of up to 94–98% with high stability of functional sponges. Although the removal efficiency of upcycled materials is less than that of similar materials derived from non-waste PU,422,423 the development of more advanced functionalisation approaches can improve it.
Only a single study has used PU sponge as a base for flexible, all-solid-state supercapacitors to create a 3D skeleton of flexible electrodes (Table 6(3)).47 The surface functionalisation with carbon materials enabled the preparation of materials featuring excellent flexibility, and its capacitance deviated seldom under various bending deformations. Furthermore, after surface modification, the porous structure of PU foams can become a perfect base for electronic applications.
Another promising application of PU films is packaging materials.424 Experiments have demonstrated the reasonable hydrophobicity of the PU surface of the accumulated sponge packing layer in air. The functional upcycling of PU waste through SiO2 coating promotes the formation of a specific packaging layer, expected to be an ideal candidate filler for gas pre-dehydration in large-scale practical applications.
Furthermore, based on the examples of commercial PU functionalisation using polymers, we suggest that upcycled PU can be applied in active biomaterials with antifouling406 or antibacterial (Table 6(2))415 properties by attaching active polymers or fatty acids (Fig. 15). However, the possibility of using upcycled plastic in medical applications is highly controversial. Plastics in a medical device are subject to several requirements and tests before use. According to ISO 10993, all plastic materials must be sterilised and tested for biocompatibility, cytotoxicity, sensitivity, and chemical and lipid resistance. Although the modern regulatory acts on medical devices (ISO 10993 and UPC classes IV) do not state the requirement of a designated source of plastic, the possibility of using functional upcycling is still open. Nevertheless, the functionalised PU with an antibacterial layer can be used in devices or coatings that are not in contact with the body. A biocompatibility test is not required, but compliance with sterilization requirements is necessary.
 Fig. 15  Comparison of functional upcycling of PU with mechanical and chemical strategies. *Qualitative analysis based on limited examples of upcycling; **Concluded from basic considerations from analysis in Section S6, ESI.† 


2.6.3. Conclusions. 
The analysis of published data indicates that both chemical and mechanical methods (Section S6 in the ESI†) are energy-intensive in terms of high temperature and pressure (Fig. 15). Moreover, the value added by mechanically recycled products is appallingly low (Section S6.1 in the ESI†). Therefore, even the low price of mechanical recycling does not ensure the sustainability of this strategy in the future. The chemical recycling of PU (Section S6.2 in the ESI†), especially glycolysis, is promising and has already been applied on a larger scale because of the reactivity of PU in condensation reactions. However, the limitations of chemical recycling include variations in process parameters for PU wastes, high temperatures, use of aggressive reagents, and low tolerance to contamination in the postconsumer materials (Section S6.2 in the ESI†). For PU, functional upcycling differs significantly from chemical upcycling in terms of the chemical composition of the surface: complete depolymerisation does not occur, and an additional functional layer is grafted (Fig. 15). Functional upcycling of PU can be considered as a diversification pathway, although further development in large-scale functionalisation is required for competitiveness.

2.7. Poly(methyl methacrylate) (PMMA)

2.7.1. General remarks. 
PMMA is a homopolymer and 0.26 Mt of it was produced in 2020 in Europe, growing at a constant rate of 4% per annum.2,425 PMMA is characterised by transparency, lightweight, durability, UV tolerance, chemical and weather resistance, safety, nontoxicity, polishability, and moderate scratch resistance. Thus, PMMA has been widely applied in the building and infrastructure sector, mostly as a substitute of transparent glass (Plexiglas®, Perspex®, and others), in addition to the automotive industry, electronics (display and touch screens), and also as lenses for glasses, houseware, leisure, sports, medical industries and others.425–429 According to MMA two project, only around 0.008 Mt PMMA is recycled annually in Europe.430,431
During application, PMMA materials are affected by environmental stresses such as moisture, oxygen/ozone, light, and temperature, which can cause chemical (hydrolysis, chain-scission, etc.) and physical changes (surface roughening, plasticization, etc.).432–436 In total, it can serve a lifespan of up to 20 years under various environmental conditions.436,437 This stability is reached by the addition of dyes, plasticisers, comonomers, UV absorbers, antioxidants, retardants, and compatibilisers.436,438,439 In spite of the low level of additives,440 they can leach from PMMA during utilisation and alter the reactivity in re-/upcycling processes. Apart from leaching, additives can contribute to additional light absorption in addition to intrinsic PMMA light absorption below 260 nm. Therefore, the fundamental aging mechanism of PMMA is photooxidation440 that occurs via the chain-scission, cross-linking, photolysis of ester group and branching.437,441 These alterations in the chemical structure varies the glass transition temperature and diminishes its toughness.437
Mechanical recycling of PMMA is seldom practiced owing to the deterioration of optical and mechanical properties442,443 and briefly discussed in Section S7.1 (ESI†). The chemical recycling approaches (Section S7.2 in ESI†) of PMMA waste involve primarily pyrolysis, gasification and carbonization, as reported in the followed reviews,29,444 based on which we further compare functional upcycling with other methods of PMMA waste recycling.


2.7.2. Functional upcycling. 
2.7.2.1. Structure of the polymer, active groups, and functionalisation agents. 
PMMA is a thermoplastic composed of (–CH2–C(CH3)(CO2CH3)–) structural units (Fig. 16). The presence of ester groups in the structure extending 1–2 Å from the backbone helps various post-modifications or surface functionalisation through preliminary hydrolysis with the formation of reactive –COOH groups.445 The functionalisation of PMMA can be performed using various chemical and physicochemical methods, either with the dissolution of the polymer (post-polymerisation modification) or without it (surface modification). Direct physical methods such as coating are restricted by the weak adhesion of the PMMA surface.446 Therefore, surface or bulk modification is more frequently adapted. Surface modification is more preferable for waste upcycling owing to the absence of an additional shaping stage, and requires lower amounts of precursors. Moreover, unlike chemical re-/upcycling, the presence of small amounts of additives in the polymer does not affect the upcycling process.
 Fig. 16  Functionalisation of PMMA: (a) direct aminolysis,447 (b) hydrolysis,448 followed by (c) amination,448 (d) esterification,449 (e) alkylation,450 (f) silanisation451 and (g) grafting/cross-linking;452 (h) modification by high reactive radicals;453 and (i) physical and chemical activation.454 
The ester group provides a wide range of possible approaches including nucleophilic substitution such as hydrolysis and aminolysis.445,455–460 The functional –CO2CH3 groups on the surface are typically less reactive in direct aminolysis than in bulk. Accordingly, more active reagents with high nucleophilicity, such as lithium amides, are applied for the modification (Fig. 16a and Table 7(4)).447,461,462 Commonly, surface methods involve two stages to introduce the desirable functional groups on the PMMA surface (Fig. 16). The first stage involves the generation of reactive carboxylic or hydroxylic species for further reactions. For –COOH groups, surface hydrolysis is widely applied through treatment with acids448,463 or bases464–466 (Fig. 16b). Ester groups can be transformed to –CH2OH by reduction with lithium aluminum hydride (LAH).445 As the introduction of –OH/–COOH groups imparts a hydrophilic property to the surface, wettability is the simplest method for analysis. Furthermore, FTIR and XPS are used to study the surface composition of functionalised PMMA.

Active functional groups can be generated via surface activation through hybrid methods, including air,467 argon,468 oxygen450,451,454,469–472 or water vapor plasma,473 UV-irradiation474–479 or ozone etching452,480 (Fig. 16i and Table 7(1, 3, 5, 6)). Although these solvent-free methods can be considered a 'greener' approach for surface modification, the selectivity of surface group transformation is relatively low, which hinders the subsequent modification stages.463
After the initial formation of carboxyl or hydroxyl groups on the surface, the second step involves the introduction of specific properties via secondary modification. For instance, hydroxylated PMMA surfaces can be modified using common organosilanes (Fig. 16f and Table 7(6)),445,451,454,472,473,481,482 by transesterification with diethanolamine (Fig. 16d and Table 7(7)),449,483 alkylation for further functionalisation (Fig. 16e and Table 7(5)),450 and radical graft polymerisation (Fig. 16g).452 The nature of these modified surfaces has been surveyed utilising contact angle measurements to assess the variations in wetting properties through XPS, FTIR, and secondary ion mass spectroscopy (SIMS).
However, the majority of the publications are dedicated to simple amination of the activated surface (–OH and –COOH) groups (Fig. 16c and Table 7(3)). These processes, realised under mild conditions, mostly at room temperature (RT), generally require EDC/NHS activation.467 In particular, amination has been performed with various compounds: ethylenediamine,448,484 hexamethylenediamine,465,485–489 cysteamine,448 cystamine,479 and aminophenylboronic acid.490 In addition, such an approach can be extended to amine-containing polymers: poly(amidoamine),463 PEI,489,491–494 aminated polyethylene glycol,466 and other aminated compounds.489
Despite the prevalence of the two-step methods, the application of diazonium salts453,495,496 or nitrene precursors497 has been developed as a direct approach to introduce the functional groups (Fig. 16h and Table 7(2)).


2.7.2.2. Application of functional materials and comparison with previously reported materials. 
The majority of the publications have dedicated their attention to the modification of commercial PMMA (Plexiglas®, Goodfellow® and other trademarks). As PMMA is primarily applied in the construction and infrastructure sector, after its end-of-life cycle, this waste does not differ substantially from its corresponding virgin materials. Thus, the methods of PMMA functionalisation should be unaffected by the initial application of the material. The methods used in functionalising virgin materials can be readily adopted for treating the PMMA waste.
Generally, waste polymers serve as a basis for the creation of sorbents for various ecotoxicants. PMMA is not an exception and its aminated waste has been applied to remove metal ions (Zn2+) (Table 7(7)).449,483 Jakóbik-Kolon et al. prepared a microporous PMMA sorbent through alkaline hydrolysis and cross-linking using diethanolamine with Ca2+ and Mg2+ ions.483 Microporous PMMA exhibited 87.7 mg g−1 adsorption capacity for Zn2+. Furthermore, this sorbent was immobilised on pectin-based beads to conduct column-scale removal of Zn2+ ions and a slightly decreased adsorption capacity was recorded (50.2 mg g−1).449 Compared to commercially available materials (clinoptilolitic zeolite and granulated activated carbon), PMMA-based sorbents achieved higher sorption capacities under neutral pH conditions (4–7).498
Owing to its wide range of physical and chemical properties, listed in Section 2.7.1, commercial PMMA is used to design various microfluidic devices, arrays, or sensors (Table 7(4)).461,487 These developments started at the beginning of the 2000s. McCarley et al. modified commercial PMMA via amination using lithiated diamine and demonstrated that the HaeIII restriction endonuclease enzyme can be covalently immobilised on the surface for the effective microdigestion of double stranded deoxyribonucleic acid (dsDNA) (Table 7(4)).461 Thereafter, Cheng et al. reported the fabrication of chips from commercial PMMA using CO2 laser patterning, followed by reduction with LAH and modification with silanes (Table 7(6)).445 The functionalisation of PMMA using organosilane with preliminary plasma irradiation improved the PMMA bonding properties for microchips with a more stable aqueous droplet flow (Table 7(6))472 or for better control of electroosmotic flow (Table 7(6)).473 The sensitivity of PMMA to light irradiation promotes the substitution of dangerous lithium compounds or high-energy plasma for softer UV-irradiation. Such a modification resulted in higher electroosmotic flow inside the microfluidic devices (Table 7(1)).476,477 The complete overview of PMMA modification for microchips and microfluidic devices has been summarised in recent reviews.499,500
The versatile chemistry of PMMA permits multistep surface functionalisation for sensing applications. As such, the aminolysis of PMMA, followed by poly(glycidyl methacrylate) grafting, has been applied as an effective approach for designing a fluorescence sensor (Table 7(4)).486 In contrast, green fluorescent protein immobilisation has been used to fabricate microfluidic antibody assay devices for detecting clinically significant concentrations of Chlamydia trachomatis specific antibodies (Fig. 17b and Table 7(3)).467 In addition to fluorescence sensors, a plasmonic sensor for Concanavalin A has been synthesized by chemisorption of Au NPs on amino- or thiol-terminated PMMA (Fig. 17a and Table 7(3)).448,463,486 Alternatively, Au NPs can be a source of electrochemical signals for nucleic acid sensing.479
 Fig. 17  Application of functionalised PMMA waste: (a) modification of PMMA optical fibers by acid hydrolysis, followed by amination and Au NP immobilisation, adapted from ref. 448; (b) air plasma-assisted –OH/–COOH group formation, followed by EDC/NHS coupling and chemisorption of the green fluorescent protein (GFP), adapted from ref. 467; and (c) – comparison of functional upcycling of PMMA with mechanical and chemical strategies. *The qualitative analysis is based on limited examples of upcycling; **Concluded from basic considerations made from analysis in Section S7 in ESI;† ***Reported only on the commercial polymers. 


2.7.3. Conclusions. 
Compared to other polymers, the chemical recycling of PMMA (Section S7.2 in ESI†) can be considered as one of the simplest processes from a technological perspective. In addition, PMMA depolymerisation proceeds more easily compared to PE, PP, and PVC without requiring expensive catalytic systems. Despite this, the high temperature of the process requires sufficient consumption of fossil fuels, resulting in a considerable carbon footprint. After depolymerisation, the derived product does not have any added value compared to virgin polymers (Fig. 17c). Therefore, alternative functional upcycling becomes extremely vital in the modern era of circular economy and sustainability. Moreover, PMMA is a near-ideal polymer for functional upcycling owing to the beneficial physical properties of its bulk material and high reactivity of the surface layers. Such a combination enables significant performance across a wide range of applications including microfluidic devices, arrays, separators, and sensors for various chemicals or biomolecules. Considering the ambitious plans for the reutilisation of polymers in Europe, the further development of upcycling methods is expected to play a key role in a sustainable market in the frame of a circular economy.

2.8. Rubber

2.8.1. General remarks. 
Synthetic rubber is an artificial elastomer derived from petroleum by-products. Styrene-butadiene rubber (SBR), prepared by copolymerisation of styrene and 1,3-butadiene, is the most widely used synthetic rubber. The widespread applicability of rubbers is related to their unique properties such as abrasion resistance and aging stability, which can be additionally modulated using additives or fillers. SBR is primarily used for tyre and footwear production,501 coatings, and adhesives.502
The global SBR capacity was 7.92 Mt in 2019, which is expected to increase to 8.57 Mt by 2024.503 More than 50% of the SBR waste is disposed of into garbage or landfills without recycling.504
In 2019, Weber and Kroll reported that ∼300 million tyres were disposed in the USA in 2017, resulting in 4 Mt of tyre waste, from which approximately 3.4 Mt was returned to the market and 16.15% was landfilled.505 From the recycled waste, ∼45% was used as rubber fuel in pulp and paper plants, cement kilns, and industrial boilers, 25% was crushed to obtain crumb rubber solutions, 8% was used for construction infrastructure work, and 3% was exported.
The major challenges of rubber re-/upcycling are associated with its complex composition and the presence of numerous additives. Another difficulty is its tendency to oxidize during storage leading to a decrease in tensile strength, elongation, and tear strength as well as discoloration and a loss of elasticity and flexibility.506 To avoid this, the inclusion of various additives such as stabilisers, antioxidants, and antiozonants in the production of tyre rubbers makes them nonbiodegradable, resistant to photochemical degradation, chemical reagents, and high temperatures.507 Moreover, the presence of double bonds and active allylic hydrogen in the polymer chains results in the intensification of the aging process when exposed to heat, oxygen, light, and ozone.508 Such processes are connected with the cleavage of polymer chains and the formation of additional cross-links and oxygen-containing groups.509 This can reduce the lifespan of rubber products by reducing its elasticity, increasing its hardness, and degrading its mechanical stability.510
The other common ingredients of tyres are natural and synthetic rubbers, carbon black, metal, textile fabric, and additives.511 In addition, tyres contain steel cord and nylon, polyester, and cellulose fibers, which must be separated from rubber when recycling waste tyres.511 Thus, recycling used tyres is a technologically, economically, and environmentally challenging task.
Based on the corresponding reviews, the common mechanical501,512,513 (Section S8.1 in ESI†) and chemical re-/upcycling514,515 methods (Section S8.2 in ESI†) for rubber are briefly discussed in Section S8 (ESI†). Accordingly, we comparatively analysed functional upcycling with other methods of rubber waste recycling.


2.8.2. Functional upcycling. 
2.8.2.1. Structure of the polymer, active groups, and functionalisation agents. 
Waste tyre rubber (WTR), primarily constituted by SBR, is a product of copolymerisation of styrene and 1,3-butadiene (Fig. 18).516
 Fig. 18  Functionalisation of rubber: (a) electrophilic halogenation using ICl or Br2, followed by amination;43,517 (b) oxidation using ozone or NOX;518–520 (c) thiol–ene coupling;521,522 and (d) sulfonation using electrophilic reaction.516,523 
The introduction of reactive functional groups into the polymer structure would enable further chemical modification to prepare a material with specific properties. The presence of double bonds and aromatic rings in the polymer backbone facilitates electrophilic addition and substitution reactions. The oxidation potential of the CC bonds for forming carboxyl or carbonyl groups is demonstrated by their treatment with nitrous oxide,518 and ozone519,520 (Fig. 18b). Moreover, rubber can be treated with Wijs' reagent for the formation of vicinal dihalogenides, followed by aminolysis using polyamines (EDA, DTA, or pentaethylenehexamine (PHA)) (Fig. 18a and Table 8(1))43,517 under microwave irradiation. Another example of an electrophilic reaction is sulfonation using H2SO4 or a H2SO4/HNO3 mixture (Fig. 18d and Table 8(3)).516,523

As an alternative approach, thiol–ene reaction can be used for the transformation of CC bonds to the corresponding thiols (Fig. 18c).521,522 For instance, the WTR surface can be modified with various polymers, e.g., poly(3-acrylamidopropyl)trimethylammonium chloride (p(APTMACl)), through an addition reaction on the CC bond in the rubber structure through –CH2–S– bond formation (Table 8(4)).524 Such approaches have been applied only to virgin polymers, but can be widened to waste products without any limitation.
Recently, a new strategy has been proposed: rubber oxidation to generate oxygen-containing species that react with trichloro(1H,1H,2H,2H-perfluorooctyl)silane (PFTS).525 Consequently, the prepared composite was applied for the design of a TENG. Although, the strategy has been reported only for PFTS, it can be readily extended to other silanes (Table 8(5)).
Owing to the inferior solubility of rubber, the characterization of upcycled materials is limited to spectroscopic techniques such as XPS or FTIR along with the elemental analysis of the bulk material.
2.8.2.2. Application of functional materials and comparison with previously reported materials. 
The materials from recycled WTR are primarily implemented as adsorbents for anions, metal ions, and dyes. Kotatha et al. demonstrated that WTR grafted with quaternary ammonium groups exhibits a much higher adsorption capacity for nitrate (NO3−) and fluoride (F−) ions compared to unmodified WTR (Fig. 19a and Table 8(1)).43,517 In addition, the adsorbent can be regenerated similarly to an ion-exchange resin. Although the modified ground rubber in the above-cited study exhibited a relatively low adsorption capacity (∼2–3.5 mg g−1), the removal ability of the modified WTR is comparable to that of commercial resins.517
 Fig. 19  Application of functionalised rubber waste: (a) adsorbent for fluoride ion removal, adapted from;43 (b) adsorbent for the removal of MB, MO, and TC, adapted from ref. 516; and (c) comparison of functional upcycling of rubber with mechanical and chemical strategies. *The qualitative analysis is based on limited examples of upcycling. **Concluded from basic considerations made from analysis in Section S8 in ESI.† 
Functionalised WTR with APTMACl, containing –N+(CH3)3 functional groups, is assessed for capturing harmful As3+ and As5+ ions (0.99 mg g−1) (Table 8(4)).524 The resulting material demonstrates excellent sorption capacity in a column adsorption method to eliminate arsenic derivatives from wastewater (0.33–0.46 mg g−1) and deliver desorption efficiency, which ensures the effectiveness and reusability of this sorbent. As an additional example, WTR is modified using lysine to eliminate Pb2+ ions (18.35 mg g−1) (Table 8(1)).526 Another path followed for producing adsorbents for Pb2+ ions is associated with the modification with MAH, which enhances the adsorption capacity (up to 144.3 mg g−1) (Table 8(2)).527 Note that the adsorption capacity of WTR modified with MAH for Pb2+ ions is larger than that of palm shell-based activated carbon,528 palm shell-activated carbon,529S. alterniflora activated carbon,530 commercial activated carbon (ST1000),530 tamarind wood-activated carbon,531 dead marine algae biomass,532 and Pseudomonas aeruginosa PU21.533
The modification with sulfuric acid results in the formation of adsorbents with a high capacity towards organic dyes and antibiotics, namely, methylene blue (MB), methyl orange (MO), and tetracycline (TC) from water (Fig. 19b and Table 8(3)).516 The maximum adsorption capacity was approximately 833, 588, and 303 mg g−1 for MB, MO and TC, respectively, which was higher than that of activated charcoal,534 bamboo charcoal,535 activated carbon,536 CNTs,537 graphene,538 graphene oxide,539,540 and MOF-235.541
A similar approach was adopted to generate adsorbents for the removal of boron (Table 8(3)).523 In this case, the WTR adsorbents were prepared by treatment with a solution of nitric acid, sulfuric acid, or a mixture of acids. At an initial concentration of 17.5 mg L−1 of boron in water, the adsorption capacity of pristine WTR, chemically-modified WTR, and nano-WTR was 16.72, 13.81, and 12.70 mg g−1, respectively. As the highest adsorption capacity was recorded for boron with WTR compared to other adsorbents reported in the literature, we can conclude that WTR is an excellent cost-effective material. Thus, the development of multifunctional adsorbents for the removal of organic pollutants from wastewater using upcycled WTR may lead to development of new technologies with sustainable economic and environmental impacts in resource-limited areas.


2.8.3. Conclusions. 
Conclusively, the common methods for rubber recycling are connected with mechanical processes or pyrolysis (Fig. 19c and Section S8 in ESI†). Despite the efficiency of such technologies, they are still far from achieving the modern trends in sustainability and resource efficiency. The functional upcycling of rubber was recently included in the arsenal of available tools, primarily focusing on the design of adsorbents, which creates an avenue for further development.

2.9. Polycarbonate (PC)

2.9.1. General remarks. 
In the past few decades, PC has emerged as a cornerstone of modern technology owing to its widespread applications in daily life and technology. PC has acquired its own stature among other plastics owing to its temperature and impact resistance, in addition to its optical properties.542,543 PC is a common polycondensation product resulting from the reaction between bisphenols (or bis-alcohols) and a carbonate source. From the vast family of PCs, poly(bisphenol A carbonate) or PC, produced from bisphenol A (BPA) via conjugation with carbonic acid derivatives, is the most commonly used PC material544–546 that is distinguished by its lightweight, ductility, transparency, thermal and flame resistance, high mechanical strength, and high stability against diverse environmental conditions.547,548 Its toughness and chemical resistance determine its applicability in electronics (smartphones and computers), optical data-storage devices (compact discs (CDs) and digital versatile disks (DVDs)), packaging (food and beverages), automotive (headlights), construction and infrastructure (panels, windows, housing and sheeting).549,550 Although BPA is a possible xenoestrogen, over 6 Mt of PC was produced in 2019.14,551 PC does not have its own resin identification code. Almost the same amount of produced PC ends up in landfills or is incinerated, thereby increasing the relevance of developing functional upcycling including CDs and DVDs as a source of waste.17,545 Landfilling or incineration is increasingly costly or inconvenient (owing to BPA release in the environment), and Mt of polymer waste end up in soils and oceans, causing major pollution, which should stimulate the rapid development of new strategies to process waste PC.
PC-based plastic waste tends to degrade after exposure to sunlight, oxygen, elevated humidity, and temperature.552,553 First, the mechanical properties and coloration are compromised after degradation.552–554 Second, PC degradation causes hydrolysis and cleavage of the primary polymer chains,554 which can be facilitated by heating under environmental conditions.553 However, the aforementioned chemical transformations related to degradation can form the basis for further functional upcycling owing to the formation of polar carbonyl, carboxyl, and alkoxyl groups.555,556
Similar to other polymers, chemical re-/upcycling of PC is hampered by the additives present in the polymer. In the case of PC, common additives include flame retardants such as decabromodiphenyl ether,557 triphenyl phosphate,558 and tetrabromobisphenol A,558 as well as plasticisers such as di-n-octyl phthalate.557 Additionally, a lacquer layer or protective coating is often required to improve its stability.559 Generally, PC is used in complex devices with a substantially high content of dyes or reflective layers (such as aluminum, gold, or silver560) which presents challenges in various re-/upcycling processes. Therefore, careful cleaning, delamination, or exfoliation561 is necessary before performing any re-/upcycling, as the presence of additives and complex mixtures significantly hinders the process.
Through various recycling strategies, the mechanical recycling of PC is less frequent than that for other plastics owing to the deterioration of mechanical properties and BPA leaching17 (Section S9.1 in ESI†). The popular mechanical recycling methods are briefly discussed in Section S9.1 (ESI†). Accordingly, the functional upcycling is further compared with other methods of PC waste recycling based on the published reviews.562–564 As discussed in Section S9.2 (ESI†), the chemical recycling of PC wastes is a rapidly growing field. Overall, hydrolysis, alcoholysis, aminolysis, pyrolysis,565 and hydrogenolysis566 are the most developed methods compared to upcycling. However, chemical re-/upcycling of industrial PC is currently at the initial stage of development owing to its harsh experimental conditions, hazardous solvents, CO2 release, low selectivity, and separation problem, among other factors. Thus, the functional upcycling is further compared with other methods of PC waste recycling based on these reviews.545,567–569


2.9.2. Functional upcycling. 
2.9.2.1. Structure of the polymer, active groups, and functionalisation agents. 
The specific reactivity of the PC chain is governed by its two main structural units – bisphenol and carbonate linker (O–C6H4–C(CH3)2–C6H4–O–CO) (Fig. 20). Compared to PP and PE, PC is more reactive owing to the presence of three available functionalisation sites: methyl groups, carbonate groups, and aromatic rings (Fig. 20).570 In the context of this review, we will discuss the methods of upcycling PC that have been tested on waste and commercial PC optical disks and sheets.
 Fig. 20  Functionalisation of PC: (a) nitrene functionalisation;571–573 (b) surface hydrolysis;574–577 (c) surface amination;548,578–581 (d) surface phosphorylation;582 (e) nitration583–588/sulfonation586/chloromethylation;588 (f) Friedel–Crafts alkylation for hyper-cross-linking;377and (g) UV-irradiation or plasma or ozone.586,589–594 
First, we consider the strategies that are used for transforming the methyl groups of PC. A long-established reagent for PC functionalisation is 1-fluoro-2-nitro-4-azidobenzene (FNAB), which serves as a source of reactive nitrene species that can attack the methyl groups of PC chains (Fig. 20a and Table 9(1)).571 Under UV-irradiation, the azido group of FNAB is transformed into a highly reactive nitrene that can bind with the inert polymer surface. Moreover, the active F-substituent in FNAB, currently a part of the polymer, remains intact. This strategy has been used to attach various proteins to the PC surface.571,572

Overall, most methods are associated with the chemical modification of carbonate moieties. Thus, the partial surface hydrolysis of the PC with SnCl2575 or NaOH576,577 results in the formation of hydroxyl and carboxyl groups (Fig. 20b and Table 9(2)) on the surface. These polar groups on PC reduce the surface energy and strengthen the hydrophilicity and adhesion. Moreover, such terminal functional groups are promising moieties for further modification.591
The carbonate groups in the polymer structure are capable of participating in nucleophilic addition reactions with N-centered nucleophiles via surface amination. Amines and diamines can react with PC under very mild conditions (in water/alcohol solutions at temperatures below 70 °C) without requiring complicated equipment (Fig. 20c and Table 9(3)),548,578–581 unlike plasma-based methods.595 The grafted amino groups represent a reactive platform for post-functionalisation: Bachand et al. introduced polyethylene glycol to create an antifouling surface through facile conversion of the amine to a maleimide.580 In addition to amines, phosphate groups have been introduced by surface phosphorylation reactions with dichloro-(N,N)-diisopropylaminophosphate (Fig. 20d and Table 9(4)).582
Electron-rich aryl moieties in PC can be readily transformed to appropriate derivatives via electrophilic substitution (Fig. 20e and Table 9(5–7)). For instance, PC sulfonation has been performed by simple PC immersion into a K2Cr2O7 and H2SO4 mixture,586 which results in the hydrophilization of the surface (Fig. 20e and Table 9(6)). The presence of S-containing groups was confirmed from the appearance of the S 2p XPS signal. Moreover, PC has been nitrated using nitric acid or its mixture with sulfuric acid at ∼50 °C (Fig. 20e and Table 9(5)).583,585–587 However, such methods are discouraged by a relatively low functionalisation degree570,588 and the use of aggressive chemicals that generate toxic greenhouse NO2. Nitrogen-containing groups can be further reduced by sodium borohydride to amino.587 As a greener alternative, PC can be alkylated by Friedel–Crafts reaction using formaldehyde dimethyl acetal for further cross-linking using Lewis acids (e.g., FeCl3 at 45 °C) (Fig. 20f and Table 9(8)).377 Finally, the same reaction was applied to introduce chloromethyl moieties using ZnCl2 as a milder Lewis acid (Fig. 20e and Table 9(7)).588
Furthermore, the PC surface can be subjected to plasma treatment for unselective conversion of the surface groups (Fig. 20g and Table 9(9)).570,591,594 In particular, new polar hydroxyl or carboxylic groups evolved upon treatment with air,596 argon,597 oxygen598 plasma. Potentially, amino groups in PC can be introduced with exposure to ammonia plasma.595 UV/ozone treatment is similar to plasma methods because it generates carboxylic and/or hydroxylic groups.590,592,593,599–601 Although plasma and UV-based methods enable the generation of polar groups that can be further transformed, upcycled PC commonly suffer from low resistance against temperature and solvents.


2.9.2.2. Application of functional materials and comparison with previously reported materials. 
Optical disks (e.g., CDs and DVDs) and other electronic waste are one of the most available sources of PC for functional upcycling. Low et al. have demonstrated that nitrated PC disks can be used as a sorbent to remove Hg2+ ions from tap, lake, and mountain water (Fig. 21a and Table 9(5)).579,583 Despite the relatively moderate adsorption capacity of PCs (0.289 mg g−1) compared to carbon materials,604 PC can be used as a sustainable sorbent for wastewater treatment. The developed chemistry of PC (Fig. 20) enables a wide range of opportunities for secondary functionalisation to attach more selective ligands, thereby rendering this direction promising for further research.
 Fig. 21  Application of functionalised PC waste: (a) adsorbent for Hg2+ ions removal, adapted from;583 and (b) comparison of functional upcycling of PC with mechanical and chemical strategies. *Qualitative analysis is based on limited examples of upcycling; **Concluded from basic considerations from analysis in Section S9, ESI;† ***Reported only on commercial polymers. 
Alternatively, waste PC has been alkylated and cross-linked to produce adsorbents for O2/N2 mixtures (Table 9(8)).377 The maximum adsorption capacities of O2/N2 are 3.68 and 2.69 mmol g−1, respectively, which are significantly less than those for hyper cross-linked PS waste.377 The inferior performance of cross-linked PC can be explained by the underdeveloped porosity and low surface area. Therefore, for adsorbent application, the surface area of the PC waste requires sufficient enhancement.
PC is an optimal material for various microfluidic applications in biomedical analytical devices. In general, plastic waste can be used to design microfluidic devices.605,606 However, in this case, the mechanical properties of the waste PC should be conserved. To date, only commercial PC has been utilised, with the most common sources being new optical disks (Table 9(3, 5, 7, 9)),548,588,590,591 filters (Table 9(5)),585 and membranes (Table 9(5, 6, 9)).586 Although wettability and adhesion are essential characteristics required for microfluidic devices, PC is neither decisively hydrophobic nor hydrophilic (Table 9(3)).603 Therefore, wettability can be varied depending on the functionalisation agent. Thus, commercial PC has been functionalised with hydrophilic amino, nitro, oxygen-containing, sulfonic groups, and polymer brushes to regulate its wettability and adhesion towards fog (Table 9(3)),548 oil (Table 9(3, 9)),589,603 bacteria (Table 9(5)),584 and cells (Table 9(5, 6, 9)).586 Alternatively, hydrophobic aliphatic chains have been affixed to PC (Table 9(3, 9)),589,602 and these methods can be further transferred to waste PC to produce certain microfluidic devices. However, biomedical analytical devices based on optical response such as colorimetric or fluorescent sensors require transparency (Table 9(5, 9)),571,574,585,587,591 which cannot be achieved in certain cases of waste PC compared to more transparent PMMA (Section 2.7).
The mechanical properties and stability of PC can be modified by functional upcycling of PC for improved durability of any functional device. The preliminary ultraviolet/ozone (UVO) treatment followed by covalent binding with Al2O3 forms a protection layer (Table 9(9))594 against acetone etching. Alternatively, UVO pre-treated PC has been modified using a silane coupling agent to improve its adhesion and mechanical properties, i.e., shear strength increased up to ∼165% compared to bare PC (Table 9(9)).592


2.9.3. Conclusions. 
Despite the availability of various strategies for recycling PC, mechanical recycling (Section S9.1 in ESI†) is limited owing to the leaching of toxic BPA (Fig. 21b). However, the intrinsic reactivity of the primary chain of PC enables the use of waste in chemical re-/upcycling processes. Both the BPA and carbonate can be recovered through hydrolysis, alcoholysis, aminolysis, and thiolysis (Section S9.2 in ESI†). However, these processes require sophisticated catalysts and high temperatures, resulting in significant carbon footprints owing to the consumption of fossil fuels in such processes. As such, CO2 emissions are common during recycling owing to the presence of a carbonyl moiety in the PC structure. Although the functional upcycling of PC is still not adequately developed, transferring the experimental procedures of chemical upcycling, as reported in ref. 578, can assist in designing new materials such as adsorbents. Additionally, the fine control of the wetting/adhesion properties of PC can be beneficial for selective interactions with specific molecules.

3. Major application fields: comparison of value-added performance with existing materials produced from non-waste sources
In the previous chapters, we discussed the application of upcycled polymers (PET, PE, PVC, PP, PS, PU, PMMA, rubber, and PC), based on which we can elucidate the major application fields of upcycled polymers.
Owing to the high impact of polymer upcycling on environmental science and technology, waste-based materials are widely used as adsorbents and membranes for removing persistent organic pollutants (POPs) and oil–water separation.45,405 Another promising direction is associated with the design of materials related to energy applications: supercapacitors,47 energy storage,56 and TENGs.56 In addition, upcycled polymers can be used for catalyst design,59,357 textile industry,99 advanced construction materials,512 and materials with controlled wettability.96,369
All these fields imply the requirement of high-value-added materials produced using complex technology to effectively fulfil real human needs. Unfortunately, the definition of added value has not been formulated thus far owing to the difficulties in calculating the actual value/price of the materials/devices synthesised from waste polymers based on the available scientific data. Therefore, to quantitatively understand the added value of waste-derived materials, we will discuss the most common application fields, considering the complexity of preparation, affordability, and actual need of these materials based on market analysis, and ultimately, compare the performances of waste-derived and non-waste-sourced materials in terms of functionality and composition. Unsurprisingly, waste-derived materials with specific performance are more affordable than those prepared from virgin resources.
3.1. Adsorbents
For all polymers, the primary application field is adsorbents or materials for oil–water separation. The most common adsorbates include oils, metal ions, dyes, and other POPs such as humic acid,49 phenols,364 anions (F− and NO3−),43,517 and TC516 (Table 10). As such, POPs are toxic chemicals that adversely affect the human health and environment on a global scale.607 Based on the analysis of market reports of 2020,608,609 the global market for adsorbents was worth USD 3.9 billion, and USD 613 million for oil–water separators,. By 2027, these markets are expected to reach USD 5.9 billion (adsorbents) and USD 790.5 million (oil–water separators) at a compound annual growth rate (CAGR) of 4–6% during the forecast period.

The market of adsorbents and oil–water separators is driven by innovations in oil and gas processing and socioeconomic advancements. These materials are applicable in multifarious industries: petrochemical, pharmaceutical, automobile, construction, and chemical industries. Moreover, stringent environmental regulations on the quality of water, soil, and food have prompted industries to develop more advanced functional materials.610 However, several adsorbents and separator materials available in the market, with beneficial aspects in removing oils and POPs, are expensive or they do not fulfil every requirement of their specified application.607 Although the common adsorbent materials (e.g., carbon, silica and alumina, and zeolites) require an average level of technological intervention for the design and manufacturing of an industrial product, more selective or ultrahigh-surface-area sorbents typically require a more advanced level of chemical technologies to improve their performance.611
All the waste polymers considered herein can be upcycled as adsorbents or oil–water separating devices, and in more instances, water purification ability is comparable or even outperforms that of the recently reported nonrecycled materials (Table 10). To compare their performance, this review considers the adsorption capacity as the fundamental indicator and the operating conditions are also specified. Upcycled PET after transesterification with hydroxyquinone46 has been designed to remove metal ions (Cu2+) from water with 62 mg g−1 maximum adsorption capacity. In comparison, fibrous adsorbents, prepared by graft copolymerisation of a methacrylic acid (MAA)/acrylamide (AAm) monomer mixture onto commercial PET fibers, exhibited two times lower adsorption capacity.612 A similar situation has been observed in the case of Zn2+ removal using upcycled PMMA,449i.e., the adsorption capacity is higher than that of well-known adsorbents,613,614 and it is more affordable than biomass-based materials.615 However, all upcycled, commercial-based, and natural-based adsorbents are highly dependable on the pH of working solutions. The common POPs, dyes, and drugs can be removed using upcycled PS45 and rubber516 in a wide pH range. In comparison, cross-linked HPF membranes from PS and PVP,384 activated carbon (Darco® G-60), activated biochar616 or sulfonated coal fly385 displayed lower adsorption capacity and a narrow working pH range.
PU waste foams and PP fabric – from a used surgical mask coated with a hydrophobic layer – have been applied for oil–water separation. Their polymer surface selectively absorbs diesel (24 g g−1)50 or crude oil (132 g g−1)405via hydrophobic–hydrophobic interactions and rejects water. Although similar adsorbents – prepared from the commercially available PU sponge411 and PP nonwoven313 fabric – exhibited comparable adsorption capacity, the final cost of the waste-derived adsorbents was lower. For example, our recent findings emphasised the benefits of MOF-coated PP fabric by calculating the expected costs of the proposed adsorbent for oil. The estimated prime cost for 1m2 was USD 4.7, which was within the price range of the commercially available fabric adsorbents.50
In contrast to PU and PP, waste PE packaging material is used for preparing superabsorbent hydrogels via surface-assisted polymerisation of AA.61 In the case of PE,61 water absorbency of PE@PAA is slightly less than that of PE/PS@PAA coupled with kaolin and halloysite nanotubes via emulsion polymerisation.617 However, on combining PS and PE waste, the water absorbency exceeds that of the nonrecycled materials and becomes applicable for saline solution.
The comparative analysis of adsorbents and oil–water separators reveals that functional materials, prepared from waste polymers, are on a similar technological level as materials prepared from commercially available films, fabrics, and sponges (Table 10). Although the performance variations of the aforementioned materials can be attributed to the surface chemistry of the waste and new polymers, our main aim is to demonstrate that waste polymer can be used for designing high-performance adsorbents and oil–water separators. In principle, functional upcycling offers the possibility of using waste for preparing adsorbents, thereby making them more affordable with the same level of the advanced production technology. However, to realise this practical application for adsorbent producers, detailed and precise procedures should be established for purification, sorting, and pre-treatment to readily access polymer waste with the required level of quality.
3.2. CO2 utilisation
The materials used for CO2 capture are highly valuable in the field of adsorbents because of environmental concerns (total emission reached 36.3 gigatonnes in 2021618). To reduce carbon emissions, the global market size of CO2 utilisation is estimated to grow from USD 3.75 billion in 2021 to USD 7 billion in 2030.619 One of the driving factors for market growth is the development of technologies that can convert CO2 into value-added chemicals such as urea and cyclic carbonates, polymers (PC and PU), and fuels (methanol and formic acid).620,621
The moderate efficiency and selectivity of commercially available CO2-capturing materials (e.g., activated carbon, zeolites, and silica) is limiting their large-scale application to reduce atmospheric CO2 concentration.622 Consequently, the development of more selective adsorbents with an extremely high surface area can overcome these limitations for more advanced chemical technologies.393,623
The most recent review of Lim et al. discussed the possibility of using waste plastics as a low-cost platform to synthesise functional materials for CO2 capture and utilisation.623 The large majority of the discussed examples are related to the chemical strategies of plastic re-/upcycling. For instance, a variety of MOFs can be prepared from PET (Section 2.1.2.1) by chemical upcycling for further CO2 capture111,624 and transformation to CO,625 or cyclic carbonates.119 Alternatively, PET can be upcycled to porous activated carbon626–628 for CO2 capture. Moreover, waste PU foam has been chemically upcycled to yield oxygen-rich and nitrogen-rich materials.629,630 The discussed strategies create new vistas for plastic re-/upcycling into functional materials with the desired adsorption capacity, porosity, stability, and selectivity for carbon capture and utilisation.
However, only a few examples can be cited forf functional upcycling, including the recent application of waste PS,365,366,378 commercial PVC270 and PE.180
First, waste PS was used as a base to prepare a mixed-matrix membrane (MMM) for CO2/N2 separation.631 Compared to other ZIF-8 based materials, the PS membrane containing ZIF-8 provided channels for CO2 transport and delivered competitive CO2-separation performance with α(CO2/N2) = 43.9.632 In contrast to noncovalent methods, the waste PS was functionalised using amino groups to increase the capacity for CO2 adsorption via chemosorption mechanism.633 Alternatively, a hyper-cross-linking strategy has been adopted for waste PS.365,366,378 The presence of relatively electron-rich phenyl rings induces dipole interactions with CO2 and increases its selectivity over N2 and CH4.634 The adsorption capacity is higher than that of commercial-based adsorbents365,378,635 and comparable to that of certain zeolites and MOFs393 (Table 11).
