Commercial PVC has been upcycled via amination of PVC and loading on silica materials to enhance its surface area with an adsorption capacity of 0.5 mmol g−1.270 Related to the adsorption of CO2, Yoshioka and Jin reported the reduction of hydrogen carbonate (as a CO2 model source) to formate using dechlorinated PVC under hydrothermal conditions in water.225 The proposed strategy represents a new green approach for CO2 conversion with simultaneous recycling of PVC waste.
In addition, commercial-grade PE–PP fibers have been upcycled using electron beam-induced polymerisation (glycidymethacrylate or N-vinylformamide) and subsequent coupling with different amines to yield a material with an adsorption capacity of up to 4.52 mmol g−1 under ambient conditions.180,639–641
The limited number of examples of functional upcycling of plastic can be broadened by considering rational strategies to enhance CO2 capture, which have been investigated in ref. 642 or related reviews. Thus, we suggest considering the (i) importance of chemical interactions between CO2 and surface groups and (ii) surface area of materials. The grafting of N-containing superbases and heteroaromatic ring systems381,643 to plastic waste can provide polar and nucleophilic active sites for CO2 chemisorption. These strategies can be implemented by aminolysis of waste PET (Section 2.1.2, Fig. 4c and Table 1(4)) and PMMA (Section 2.7.2, Fig. 16a and Table 7(4)), azidation/amination/cyanation of PVC (Section 2.3.2, Fig. 8a, b, e and Table 3(2, 6)), surface hydrolysis of PU (Section 2.6.2, Fig. 14b and Table 6(4)), formation of hydrozones of PS (Section 2.5.2, Fig. 12d and Table 5(3)), halogenation/amination/thiol–ene coupling of rubber (Section 2.8.2, Fig. 18a, c and Table 8(1)) and amination/oxidation/halogenation of PE/PP (Sections S2.2.2 and S2.4.2, Fig. 6b, d, f, 10f and Tables 2(5, 6) and 4(5)).
To increase the surface area as the secondary significant factor, plastic waste can be physically recycled to fibers/aerogels (applicable for PET, PS, PC) or already fibrous materials (PP masks or hospital bed sheets50) with high exterior surface areas, minimal pressure dips, and flexibility can be utilised.644 Alternatively, plastic-based composites with high-surface-area materials, e.g., MOFs645 or mesoporous silica bear potential for CO2-related applications.
3.3. Energy-related devices
Another common application field of upcycled polymers is energy-related systems, especially supercapacitors47,322 and TENGs (Table 12).56,525 Although supercapacitors store and release energy by reversible adsorption/desorption of ions at the interface, a TENG is a type of mechanical energy harvester that converts the kinetic energy generated from vibrations (including the motion of human, machine, and automobile) into electric energy.646,647 Both these types of charge-storage devices are crucial for a sustainable future. Owing to the urgent need of replacing fossil fuels with renewable sources, energy-storage systems that capture and store electricity from renewable sources have attracted attention.646 This has been proven by the estimated five-fold increase in global supercapacitor market size, growing at a CAGR of 23.5% from 2022 to 2030.648 The energy device market is driven by their increasing penetration in hybrid vehicles, energy harvesting, and consumer electronics products. The related driving factors include superior power density and longer product life-cycle, regardless of the capacitor charge cycle. Simultaneously, the high initial costs of materials and the lack of knowledge of supercapacitors act as the limiting factors.649,650 Therefore, the preparation of low-cost supercapacitors with high efficiency can enhance their scale of applicability. TENGs have not yet matured to the commercialization stage, but significant progress has been achieved since their discovery in 2012 by Wang et al.651 The use of waste polymers for designing TENGs can accelerate their development because of the initial low cost of these devices.

Among the overviewed polymers waste, PU sponge and PP fabrics have been used for supercapacitor application (Table 12). An additional conductive layer has been deposited on the polymer base: CNTs@CNFs@PANI on PU47 and Au and polypyrrole microcones on PP.322 Overall, the capacitance values suggest the similar performance level of the waste-derived supercapacitors, regardless of the direct comparison with their analogues from nonrecycled PU and PP,652–654 which manifest a complex behaviour owing to the dissimilar current density, electrolyte, and other parameters.
TENGs are another type of devices compared to supercapacitors, which harvest and convert vibration energy into electric energy. The increased surface-charge density is an essential factor that can be manipulated according to the degree of surface roughness and chemical properties. In the case of PET,56 waste-based aerogel provides a surface roughness paired with a dense amine-rich surface to reach a maximum voltage of 67.7 V at a power density of 5.47 mW m−2. The fluorinated TENG, prepared from waste rubber, exhibited exceptional characteristics: 265 V maximum voltage at 75 μA current.525 The rubber waste-based TENG can directly drive 100 commercial light emitting diodes without any energy-storing device. These TENGs demonstrate superior performance compared to commercial material-based devices,656,657 including virgin rubber.658 Therefore, we can conclude that waste-derived TENGs are relatively low-cost devices for sustainable energy harvesting and provide significant opportunities for extending waste utilisation (Table 12).
Other application fields (e.g., catalysts, sensors, textiles, building materials, and antibacterial materials) of functionally upcycled polymers are more diversified and determined by the initial properties of the polymers (Sections S2.1–S2.9). Simultaneously, the functionality of the prepared materials is defined by the surface chemistry; therefore, effective functional upcycling is impossible without a diverse toolbox of functionalisation techniques, which have been detailed in Sections S2.1–S2.9. Thus, to construct a functional device from waste polymers, the initial polymer properties and their surface chemistry should be equivocally considered. However, the limited number of existing reports on functional upcycling primarily revealed that such devices are not inferior in terms of the main indicators of the given applications. Despite all the advantages of using polymer waste such as strong market competitiveness and application potential, a more detailed cost analysis of each case is required to estimate the profitability of upcycling in industrial applications.
4. Conclusions and outlook
4.1. Conclusions
In conclusion, we can explicitly confirm that the upcycling of polymer wastes is emerging as a central strategy in the modern world. Currently, the society is experiencing widespread environmental and economic challenges associated with polymer lifespan after implementation in technological processes or daily life. For a sustainable future, scientific and engineering communities have to encounter the challenges posed by ‘polymer waste’ management and processing using the recent progress in advanced chemistry and materials science. In this regard, significant effort has been invested during the past decades, although only less than 10% of overall plastic is recycled or upcycled currently.659 Among the wide range of methods employed for polymer processing and (re)utilisation, sufficient progress has been achieved during the previous years in chemical and mechanical recycling compared to the traditional borderline landfilling and incineration. Recently, chemical recycling methods have been extended for upcycling, defined by Sardon14 as ‘the use of plastic waste as a feedstock for the synthesis of value-added products, being polymers, molecules or materials’.
In the framework of this review, we clarified the significant difference between chemical and functional upcycling. In chemical upcycling, the transformations of the main polymer chain are predominant, whereas in functional upcycling, the post-polymerisation functionalisation and surface modification methods are primarily applied. Consequently, 'functional upcycling' is introduced as a method for functionalising waste polymer surfaces or primary polymer chains without sufficient chain destruction to produce a new upcycled material with an added value. Although the research has been ongoing in this field over the past 5–10 years, the concept of 'functional upcycling' has not been formulated earlier with precise principles. Therefore, this strategy is extremely novel and under-investigated. This research comprehensively reviewed the nine most common large-scale polymers: PET, PE, PVC, PP, PS, PU, PMMA, rubber, and PC. These polymers have already been processed by functional upcycling or can be potentially upcycled by leveraging a wide chemical toolbox. For the unperturbed introduction of functional upcycling, we first provided the general information of the production, application, and recycling volumes of each polymer. Thereafter, we briefly overviewed the mechanical and chemical re-/upcycling methods (mainly presented in ESI†), including the recent trends with relevant references to specific reviews. Finally, we discussed the functionalisation of polymers in terms of the structure of each polymer, chemical groups involved, and the reagent toolbox. This information is imminently essential for scientists pursuing the development of the functional upcycling strategy. Where applicable, we overviewed the utilisation of the value-added upcycled material and compared the performance with previously reported materials from non-waste sources. The strategy of functional upcycling is entirely multidisciplinary, because knowledge in organic chemistry, surface analysis, material engineering, and the specific application field is required. Therefore, in this review, we performed systematization of various aspects of functional upcycling. In conclusion, for each polymer, we compared the mechanical, chemical, and functional re-/upcycling based on the required amount of energy, cost, environmental effects, and value-added cost.
4.2. Challenges and outlook
Generally, both chemical and mechanical methods are energy-intensive because the entire volume of the waste polymer should be processed (based on analysis in ESI†). In the case of mechanical recycling, the added value is substantially low owing to the relatively low-cost application of waste polymers in the construction and infrastructure sector (Sections S1.1–S9.1 in ESI†). Frequently, chemical methods are promising and have already been applied on a larger scale (Sections S1.2–S9.2 in ESI†). However, these techniques encounter several limitations such as high temperatures, use of aggressive reagents, solvents, and complicated catalysts in large volumes, and low tolerance to contamination. Therefore, chemical strategies are energy-intensive and yield significantly negative environmental effects by releasing large quantities of toxic and hazardous substances into the environment.660 Moreover, such approaches are poorly fitted to the demands of the modern circular economy. As such, functional upcycling differs meaningfully from chemical upcycling in terms of chemical composition: complete depolymerisation does not occur and additional functional groups are grafted. In the case of surface grafting without plastic dissolution, reagents and energy are required in smaller amounts, and CO2 is almost not generated during this process. However, if dissolution is required for both chemical and functional methods (lower solvent volume is required), the sustainability of solvents is essential for fulfilling the principles of circular economy and green chemistry. Commonly, chlorinated or dipolar aprotic solvents such as N-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP) and N,N-dimethylformamide (DMF), are used in the dissolution of plastic,661 which are often hazardous to human health and the environment. Sustainable solvents can constitute a comprehensive solution; for instance, methyl lactate, ethyl lactate, Cyrene™, PolarClean®, natural organic carbonates, ionic liquids, vegetable oil, and others, have reported adequate compatibility with various polymers.662 However, most current research focusing on reaction solvents is indirectly diminishing the developmental progress of solvents used in plastic re-/upcycling.662 Despite these challenges, functionally upcycled materials with a high added value have been successfully used as adsorbents, catalysts, and electrode materials for energy storage as well as sensors. Importantly, we demonstrated that the specific performance of upcycled materials is often within or higher than the level of similar materials prepared from virgin feedstock. All these advantages make functional upcycling a promising diversification approach to the common polymer waste post-processing methods.
However, the functional upcycling strategy is currently at the beginning of the developmental track and several challenges need to be solved in the near future. First, despite the abundance and reactivity of PMMA and PVC, the number of reported examples is limited. In addition, the high rigidity of PE and PP chains limits the ease of implementing functional upcycling realization. Therefore, in addition to rare examples, this review reports on cases of functionalisation of commercial polymers, which can be further adapted to plastic waste. Moreover, numerous types of plastic waste have not been tested so far. In particular, there is a genuine need to upcycle epoxy composites, stimuli-responsive polymers, and special polymers such as polyether ether ketone, polysulfones, and highly reinforced composites. The polymer blends commonly used in both postconsumer and post-industrial waste sources are widely applied in packaging, such as PP/PE,663 PET/PVC,664 PMMA/PVC,665 and PET/rubber.666 To realise functional upcycling of such polymer mixtures, specific chemical properties of each polymer should be considered. Another promising development pathway is the combination of various waste sources; for instance, the TA from PET can be combined with the aluminum derived from waste lithium-batteries to synthesise MIL-53(Al).667
According to the findings of this review, adsorbents and energy-storage devices are the most common application fields for upcycled polymers. However, a further increase in the added value is expected because plastic waste can be upcycled to more advanced materials such as selective adsorbents or separators for gases, energy production devices, sensors, and microfluidic systems. However, as these devices do not require large-scale upcycling, functional upcycling is more suitable for low and average volumes of plastic waste. Despite the utilisation of plastic wastes in various fields such as PET (antibacterial materials, adsorbents, textile, and energy harvesting materials), further diversification to advanced applications is anticipated. However, this context raises the question of precisely defining the ‘added value’. Regardless of an intuitive understanding as ‘additional valuable function of the materials, which is higher than that obtained from monomers produced by depolymerisation’, a more precise definition and relevant metrics are required. For example, life-cycle assessment, techno-economic assessment, and material flow analysis can aid in realistically evaluating the economic feasibility of functional upcycling.
Another challenge pertains to transferring certain chemical recycling strategies on post-polymerisation and surface techniques and discovering the functionalisation for polymer wastes from the relevant implementation of developed methods. From the perspective of sustainability, such an experimental pathway is definitely appealing because of the sufficient economy of energy and reagents. The replenishment of the toolbox for controlling the surface properties of waste will open a new perspective in the application of functionally upcycled materials. In addition to transferring the chemical strategies, scaling up from the laboratory examination to the pilot scale of the existing and new suggested methods can be appealing for adapting functional upcycling to technology. In this case, large scale post-industrial waste will be involved in functional upcycling.
Although functional upcycling cannot substitute mechanical and chemical re-/upcycling operating large-tonnage plastic waste streams (ESI†), we believe that the functional upcycling strategy can combine the major advantages of chemical re-/upcycling (high added value) and mechanical recycling (low environmental effects) to create new functional materials and solve global challenges. We do not intend to impose this strategy as a panacea; instead, we encourage a mindful analysis of each polymer structure and its properties to select the optimal strategy of re-/upcycling, where functional upcycling is a promising alternative to existing methods.

Table Content:

Olga Guselnikova
	Dr Olga Guselnikova received her PhD degree in chemistry from the University of Chemistry and Technology Prague (Czech Republic) and Tomsk Polytechnic University (Russia) in 2019. She is a fellow of Japan Society for the Promotion of Science at the National Institute for Materials Science, Japan, and a sub-group leader of “functional materials” at Tomsk Polytechnic University. Her research interests are related to surface chemistry for functional materials. This means that she is applying her background in organic chemistry to materials science: plasmonic and polymer surfaces are hybridized with organic molecules to create high-performance elements and devices.

Yusuke Yamauchi
	Yusuke Yamauchi received his PhD degree from Waseda University in 2007 and subsequently established his research group at the National Institute of Materials Science (NIMS). In 2017, he moved to the University of Queensland where he currently serves as a professor and senior group leader at the School of Chemical Engineering and the Australian Institute for Bioengineering and Nanotechnology (AIBN). He is also a distinguished professor at Nagoya University and an ERATO Research Director at the JST-ERATO Yamauchi Materials Space-Tectonics. His area of expertise is inorganic material nanoarchitectonics.

Rabah Boukherroub
	Dr Rabah Boukherroub received his PhD degree in chemistry from the University Paul Sabatier in Toulouse, France. He is currently a research director at the French National Center for Scientific Research (CNRS) and a group leader at the Institute of Electronics, Microelectronics and Nanotechnology (IEMN), Lille, France. He is an Editor for ACS Applied Materials & Interfaces. His research interests are in the area of functional materials, surface chemistry, and photophysics of semiconductor/metal nanostructures with emphasis on biosensors, nanomedicine, photocatalysis and electrocatalysis. He is also a co-founder of BioNaMeris, a biotech firm specialized in sustainable aquaculture.

Pavel Postnikov
	Prof. Pavel S. Postnikov completed his doctorate in organic chemistry with theoretical and practical insights in diazonium chemistry at the Tomsk Polytechnic University, Russia, in 2011. In 2021, he successfully passed the habilitation procedure at the Novosibirsk Institute of Organic Chemistry, RAS, Russia (organic and physical chemistry). He serves as an associate editor of Chemistry Africa journal. His research interests are plastic recycling, composite materials, surface chemistry, plasmon catalysis, hypervalent iodine, and diazonium salts.
